The Land of Transoxiana: A Cradle for Maturidi Scholarship
Describes Transoxiana's geography and role as a nurturing ground for Maturidi scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the region of Transoxiana provided a conducive environment for the development of Maturidi theology.

Core Idea
Transoxiana was a fertile homeland for the Sunni Maturidi tradition.

Before we meet Abu al-Mu‘in al-Nasafi, we must understand the land that shaped him. Transoxiana was not just a geographical region; it was a cradle of scholarship and stability. This passage introduces the region's features and its role in nurturing the Maturidi school.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage describing Transoxiana
Read the first part of the source where the region is described. Note the cities and geography mentioned.
2
Identify the characteristics of the region
Look for words that describe the fertility, stability, and political support for scholars.
The passage uses words like 'fertile soil', 'good people', 'stability', and 'rulers encourage scholars'.
3
Connect to the Maturidi school
The passage explicitly states that Transoxiana was a prosperous birthplace for the Maturidis. Reflect on how environment can shape theological schools.
4
Consider the impact of the Khwarazm Shah invasion
The passage notes that the region was destroyed around 600 AH. Think about how historical events affect scholarly traditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العلم، وهي على مدرج بخاري، وبلخ، وبينها وبين جيحون مفازة لا جبل فيها، ولها نهر واحد يجري في وسط المدينة، وهي من بلاد ما وراء النهر، وهي مجموعة من الأقاليم تشمل فرغانة والشاس وأشروسنة، وكاشفر، وبخاري، وسمرقند، وفي غربيها تقع خراسان، وقد ظلت هذه المنطقة في استقرار وأمن إلى أن ملكها خوارزم شاه في حدود سنة ٦٠٠ه فخربها. ولقد كانت منطقة ما وراء النهر، منشأ، ومعاشا صالحا لأهل السنة والجماعة من الماتريدية، فتربتها خصبة وأهلها طيبون والظروف السياسية التي كانت تشملها تميل إلى الاستقرار، وحكامها يشجعون العلم والعلماء، ويقربونهم منهم. وفي هذا الحقل عاش أبو المعين ميمون النسفي. والسؤال الآن على من تلقي النسفي العلم؟ وما هي صلته بالأحناف والماتريدية؟ وللإجابة نقول : إن المدرسة التي نما، وترعرع في أحضانها، وتتلمذ فيها كانت أسرته، فأبوه محمد بن محمد بن معتمد يروي عنه النسفي كتاب العالم والمتعلم - لأبي حنيفة كما يدل النص التالي : قال أبو الحسن على بن خليل الدمشقي: أنبأنا أبو الحسن برهان الدين على بن الحسن البلخي عن أبي المعين ميمون بن محمد النسفي عن أبيه عن عبد الكريم بن موسى البزدوي عن الإمام أبي حنيفة. ثم جده الثاني هو أبو المعالي معتمد بن محمد يروي عن أبيه، ويأخذ العلم عن أبي سهل الأسفراييني، ويروي عنه كتاب - أخبار مكة ـ فإذا ما عرفنا أن معتمد هذا قد ولد سنة ٣٥٩هـ ومات سنة نيف وثلاثين وأربعمائة، وأضفنا إلى ذلك أن ولادة أبي المعين ميمون النسفي كانت في هذه الفترة، وأن أسرته يأخذ اللاحق عن السابق؛ خرجنا بنتيجة مؤداها أن أبا المعين تتلمذ على أبيه وعلى جده الثاني، أو كتبه على الأقل، وإذا أضفنا إلى ذلك أيضا أن أبا البديع أحمد بن أخ لمعتمد بن محمد، وعرفنا أنه كان بارعا في الفقه، وأن جدهما هو مكحول بن الفضل النسفي له كتاب اللؤلؤيات، وهذا الكتاب كما يقول عنه القرشي . مجلد ضخم رأيته وملكته بحمد الله، وله كتاب اللباب، وكتاب الشماع الذي ذكر فيه رأي أبي حنيفة فيمن رفع يديه عند الركوع، وعند رفع الرأس؛ خرجنا بنتيجة مؤداها أن علم أبي المعين ميمون امتداد طبيعي لأسرة عالمة، وأن تلمذته كانت بالدرجة الأولى على كتب أجداده، وما عدا ذلك فلم أعثر في كتب التراجم على ذكر لأساتذة آخرين تتلمذ عليهم.
Knowledge, and it is on the road of Bukhara and Balkh, and between them and Jayhun is a desert with no mountain in it, and it has one river that flows through the middle of the city. It is from the lands of Transoxiana, a collection of regions including Farghana, al-Shash, Ushrusana, Kashfar, Bukhara, and Samarkand, and to its west lies Khurasan. This region remained in stability and security until Khwarazm Shah conquered it around the year 600 AH and destroyed it. The region of Transoxiana was a prosperous birthplace and dwelling for the Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a of the Maturidis; its soil was fertile, its people good, the political circumstances tended toward stability, and its rulers encouraged knowledge and scholars and brought them close. In this field lived Abu al-Mu'in Maymun al-Nasafi. Now the question: from whom did al-Nasafi receive knowledge? And what is his connection to the Hanafis and Maturidis? For the answer we say: The school in which he grew up, was nurtured, and studied was his family. His father Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Mu'tamad – al-Nasafi narrates from him the book al-‘Alim wal-Muta‘allim of Abu Hanifa, as the following text indicates: Abu al-Hasan ‘Ali ibn Khalil al-Dimashqi said: Abu al-Hasan Burhan al-Din ‘Ali ibn al-Hasan al-Balkhi informed us, from Abu al-Mu'in Maymun ibn Muhammad al-Nasafi, from his father, from ‘Abd al-Karim ibn Musa al-Bazdawi, from Imam Abu Hanifa. Then his second grandfather is Abu al-Ma‘ali Mu‘tamad ibn Muhammad; he narrates from his father, and took knowledge from Abu Sahl al-Isfarayini, and from him is narrated the book Akhbar Makka. So when we know that this Mu‘tamad was born in the year 359 AH and died around thirty and four hundred [430 AH?], and we add to that that the birth of Abu al-Mu‘in Maymun al-Nasafi was in this period, and that his family takes the later from the earlier, we conclude that Abu al-Mu‘in studied under his father and his second grandfather, or at least under their books. And if we also add that Abu al-Badi‘ Ahmad, the son of the brother of Mu‘tamad ibn Muhammad, and we know that he was proficient in fiqh, and that their grandfather is Mak-hul ibn al-Fadl al-Nasafi, who has the book al-Lu'lu'iyyat – and this book, as al-Qurashi says about it, is a huge volume that I have seen and owned, praise be to God – and he has the book al-Lubab, and the book al-Shuma‘ in which he mentioned Abu Hanifa's opinion on raising the hands during bowing and raising the head; we conclude that the knowledge of Abu al-Mu‘in Maymun is a natural extension of a scholarly family, and that his education was primarily through the books of his ancestors, and beyond that I have not found in the biographical works mention of other teachers under whom he studied.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage begins by describing the region of Transoxiana, its cities and stability, and how it was a fertile ground for the Maturidi school. It then introduces Abu al-Mu’in al-Nasafi and poses two questions: from whom did he receive knowledge, and what is his connection to the Hanafis and Maturidis? The answer emphasizes that his family was his primary school. It lists his father Muhammad and his second grandfather Abu al-Ma’ali Mu‘tamad, providing a specific chain of transmission for the book al-‘Alim wal-Muta‘allim that goes back to Abu Hanifa. It also mentions other relatives like Abu al-Badi‘ Ahmad and the scholarly works of Makhul ibn al-Fadl, concluding that Nasafi’s education was a natural extension of his learned family and that no external teachers are recorded.

Key Terms

الماتريدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maturidi school developed without any environmental factors.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that Transoxiana's stability and patronage were crucial for its growth.

?

?

?

Nasafi's Scholarly Lineage: Education Within the Family
Explains Nasafi's education through his family and the chain of transmission to Abu Hanifa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Nasafi's primary education came from his family, specifically his father and grandfather, and the chain connecting him to Abu Hanifa.

Core Idea
Nasafi's knowledge was a natural extension of his learned family.

Having placed Nasafi in Transoxiana, we now ask: who taught him? The answer may surprise you—his family. This passage traces his genealogy of learning, including a direct chain to Abu Hanifa, and shows that his education was primarily domestic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about Nasafi's teachers
Read the second part of the source where the question of teachers is addressed.
2
Identify the family members mentioned
Note the father Muhammad ibn Muhammad and the second grandfather Abu al-Ma‘ali Mu‘tamad.
3
Analyze the chain for al-‘Alim wal-Muta‘allim
Follow the chain: from Nasafi to his father to Abd al-Karim al-Bazdawi to Abu Hanifa.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that Nasafi studied under his father and grandfather, or through their books, and no other teachers are recorded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العلم، وهي على مدرج بخاري، وبلخ، وبينها وبين جيحون مفازة لا جبل فيها، ولها نهر واحد يجري في وسط المدينة، وهي من بلاد ما وراء النهر، وهي مجموعة من الأقاليم تشمل فرغانة والشاس وأشروسنة، وكاشفر، وبخاري، وسمرقند، وفي غربيها تقع خراسان، وقد ظلت هذه المنطقة في استقرار وأمن إلى أن ملكها خوارزم شاه في حدود سنة ٦٠٠ه فخربها. ولقد كانت منطقة ما وراء النهر، منشأ، ومعاشا صالحا لأهل السنة والجماعة من الماتريدية، فتربتها خصبة وأهلها طيبون والظروف السياسية التي كانت تشملها تميل إلى الاستقرار، وحكامها يشجعون العلم والعلماء، ويقربونهم منهم. وفي هذا الحقل عاش أبو المعين ميمون النسفي. والسؤال الآن على من تلقي النسفي العلم؟ وما هي صلته بالأحناف والماتريدية؟ وللإجابة نقول : إن المدرسة التي نما، وترعرع في أحضانها، وتتلمذ فيها كانت أسرته، فأبوه محمد بن محمد بن معتمد يروي عنه النسفي كتاب العالم والمتعلم - لأبي حنيفة كما يدل النص التالي : قال أبو الحسن على بن خليل الدمشقي: أنبأنا أبو الحسن برهان الدين على بن الحسن البلخي عن أبي المعين ميمون بن محمد النسفي عن أبيه عن عبد الكريم بن موسى البزدوي عن الإمام أبي حنيفة. ثم جده الثاني هو أبو المعالي معتمد بن محمد يروي عن أبيه، ويأخذ العلم عن أبي سهل الأسفراييني، ويروي عنه كتاب - أخبار مكة ـ فإذا ما عرفنا أن معتمد هذا قد ولد سنة ٣٥٩هـ ومات سنة نيف وثلاثين وأربعمائة، وأضفنا إلى ذلك أن ولادة أبي المعين ميمون النسفي كانت في هذه الفترة، وأن أسرته يأخذ اللاحق عن السابق؛ خرجنا بنتيجة مؤداها أن أبا المعين تتلمذ على أبيه وعلى جده الثاني، أو كتبه على الأقل، وإذا أضفنا إلى ذلك أيضا أن أبا البديع أحمد بن أخ لمعتمد بن محمد، وعرفنا أنه كان بارعا في الفقه، وأن جدهما هو مكحول بن الفضل النسفي له كتاب اللؤلؤيات، وهذا الكتاب كما يقول عنه القرشي . مجلد ضخم رأيته وملكته بحمد الله، وله كتاب اللباب، وكتاب الشماع الذي ذكر فيه رأي أبي حنيفة فيمن رفع يديه عند الركوع، وعند رفع الرأس؛ خرجنا بنتيجة مؤداها أن علم أبي المعين ميمون امتداد طبيعي لأسرة عالمة، وأن تلمذته كانت بالدرجة الأولى على كتب أجداده، وما عدا ذلك فلم أعثر في كتب التراجم على ذكر لأساتذة آخرين تتلمذ عليهم.
Knowledge, and it is on the road of Bukhara and Balkh, and between them and Jayhun is a desert with no mountain in it, and it has one river that flows through the middle of the city. It is from the lands of Transoxiana, a collection of regions including Farghana, al-Shash, Ushrusana, Kashfar, Bukhara, and Samarkand, and to its west lies Khurasan. This region remained in stability and security until Khwarazm Shah conquered it around the year 600 AH and destroyed it. The region of Transoxiana was a prosperous birthplace and dwelling for the Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a of the Maturidis; its soil was fertile, its people good, the political circumstances tended toward stability, and its rulers encouraged knowledge and scholars and brought them close. In this field lived Abu al-Mu'in Maymun al-Nasafi. Now the question: from whom did al-Nasafi receive knowledge? And what is his connection to the Hanafis and Maturidis? For the answer we say: The school in which he grew up, was nurtured, and studied was his family. His father Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Mu'tamad – al-Nasafi narrates from him the book al-‘Alim wal-Muta‘allim of Abu Hanifa, as the following text indicates: Abu al-Hasan ‘Ali ibn Khalil al-Dimashqi said: Abu al-Hasan Burhan al-Din ‘Ali ibn al-Hasan al-Balkhi informed us, from Abu al-Mu'in Maymun ibn Muhammad al-Nasafi, from his father, from ‘Abd al-Karim ibn Musa al-Bazdawi, from Imam Abu Hanifa. Then his second grandfather is Abu al-Ma‘ali Mu‘tamad ibn Muhammad; he narrates from his father, and took knowledge from Abu Sahl al-Isfarayini, and from him is narrated the book Akhbar Makka. So when we know that this Mu‘tamad was born in the year 359 AH and died around thirty and four hundred [430 AH?], and we add to that that the birth of Abu al-Mu‘in Maymun al-Nasafi was in this period, and that his family takes the later from the earlier, we conclude that Abu al-Mu‘in studied under his father and his second grandfather, or at least under their books. And if we also add that Abu al-Badi‘ Ahmad, the son of the brother of Mu‘tamad ibn Muhammad, and we know that he was proficient in fiqh, and that their grandfather is Mak-hul ibn al-Fadl al-Nasafi, who has the book al-Lu'lu'iyyat – and this book, as al-Qurashi says about it, is a huge volume that I have seen and owned, praise be to God – and he has the book al-Lubab, and the book al-Shuma‘ in which he mentioned Abu Hanifa's opinion on raising the hands during bowing and raising the head; we conclude that the knowledge of Abu al-Mu‘in Maymun is a natural extension of a scholarly family, and that his education was primarily through the books of his ancestors, and beyond that I have not found in the biographical works mention of other teachers under whom he studied.

Nasafi's Chain to Abu Hanifa and Maturidi
Traces the scholarly chains from Nasafi to Abu Hanifa and to Maturidi through Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi and Maturidi are connected through a shared scholarly chain originating from Abu Hanifa via Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani.

Core Idea
Scholarly chains reveal the shared roots of Hanafi and Maturidi traditions.

This passage traces the intellectual lineage of Imam al-Nasafi, showing how he and Imam al-Maturidi both trace their knowledge back to Abu Hanifa through a common intermediary, Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani. Understanding these chains helps us see the continuity and authority of the Hanafi-Maturidi school.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the names and the sequence of transmission.
2
Trace the first chain
Identify the chain from Nasafi's grandfather Mu‘tamad back to Abu Hanifa. List the scholars in order.
The chain goes: Mu‘tamad → his father → his father → Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani → Muhammad ibn al-Hasan → Abu Hanifa.
3
Trace the second chain
Identify the chain from Imam al-Maturidi back to Abu Hanifa. Note the different intermediaries before reaching Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani.
The chain goes: Maturidi → Abu Nasr Ahmad al-‘Iyadi → Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Ishaq al-Juzjani → Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani → Muhammad ibn al-Hasan → Abu Hanifa.
4
Compare the chains
Observe that both chains converge at Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani and then go to Abu Hanifa. This shows a common source and a split after al-Juzjani.
The passage mentions 'two branches' at Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهنا نصل إلى النقطة الثانية، وهي الصلة بينه وبين الأحناف، والماتريدية، ولبيان ذلك أقول : إن أبا المعالي معتمدًا بن محمد النسفي الجد الثاني لأبي المعين قد روي عن أبيه أبي المعين محمد بن مكحول، الذي روى بدوره عن أبيه مكحول النسفي، والأخير أخذ العلم عن أبي سليمان الجوزجاني عن محمد بن الحسن الشيباني عن أبي حنيفة، فإذا وضعنا بمقابلة معتمد بن محمد النسفي الإمام أبا منصور الماتريدي نجد أن الأخير قد أخذ العلم عن أبي نصر أحمد العياضي عن أبي بكر أحمد بن إسحاق الجوزجاني عن أبي سليمان الجوزجاني عن محمد بن الحسن عن الإمام أبي حنيفة. المنبع إذا كان أبا حنيفة، وعند أبي سليمان الجوزجاني وجد فرعان: أحدهما ...
And here we reach the second point, which is the connection between him and the Hanafis and Maturidis. To clarify that I say: Abu al-Ma‘ali Mu‘tamad ibn Muhammad al-Nasafi, the second grandfather of Abu al-Mu‘in, narrated from his father Abu al-Mu‘in Muhammad ibn Mak-hul, who in turn narrated from his father Mak-hul al-Nasafi, and the latter took knowledge from Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani from Abu Hanifa. When we place opposite Mu‘tamad ibn Muhammad al-Nasafi the Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, we find that the latter took knowledge from Abu Nasr Ahmad al-‘Iyadi from Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Ishaq al-Juzjani from Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan from Imam Abu Hanifa. The source then is Abu Hanifa, and at Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani two branches are found: one of them...
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage addresses the second point: the connection between Nasafi and the Hanafis and Maturidis. It presents two chains: one from Nasafi’s grandfather Mu‘tamad back to Abu Hanifa via Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani, and another from Imam al-Maturidi back to Abu Hanifa also via Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani. The conclusion is that both share the same source (Abu Hanifa) and that Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani is the common link from which two branches emerge. The passage ends mid-sentence at the window boundary, but the core argument is complete.

Correction: The passage shows they have parallel chains that converge at Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani, not that Nasafi studied directly under Maturidi.

Nasafi's Birth, Family, and Early Education
Describes Nasafi's birth era, family scholars, and his primary education sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi's family background shaped his early theological education and the primary sources of his knowledge.

Core Idea
Nasafi's knowledge was a natural extension of his learned family.

This lesson introduces you to the early life of Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi, a key figure in Maturidi theology. The passage reveals that he was born into a scholarly family and that his education came primarily from his father and grandfather, and their books. Understanding this helps us appreciate the intellectual lineage that shaped his works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the biographical details and the flow of the argument.
Focus on the key names and their relationships.
2
Identify the key figures
Note the names mentioned: Mu'tamid (a relative), Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi, Abu al-Badi' Ahmad, and Makhul ibn al-Fadl al-Nasafi. Understand their family connections.
Makhul is the grandfather, and Abu al-Badi' is a nephew of Mu'tamid.
3
Trace the educational lineage
The author concludes that Abu al-Mu'in studied under his father and grandfather, or at least through their books. This shows a family tradition of knowledge transmission.
The author also mentions books authored by Makhul: al-Lu'lu'iyyat, al-Lubab, and al-Shumma'.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by stating that Abu al-Mu'in's knowledge is a natural extension of his learned family, and no other teachers are recorded. Consider why this might be significant.
This does not mean he had no teachers at all, but that his primary education was within the family.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معتمدا هذا قد ولد سنة ٣٥٩هـ ومات سنة نيف وثلاثين وأربعمائة(۱)، وأضفنا إلى ذلك أن ولادة أبي المعين ميمون النسفي كانت في هذه الفترة، وأن أسرته يأخذ اللاحق عن السابق ؛ خرجنا بنتيجة مؤداها أن أبا المعين تتلمذ على أبيه وعلى جده الثاني، أو كتبه على الأقل، وإذا أضفنا إلى ذلك أيضا أن أبا البديع أحمد بن أخ لمعتمد بن محمد، وعرفنا أنه كان بارعا في الفقه(۲)، وأن جدهما هو مكحول بن الفضل النسفي له كتاب اللؤلؤيات، وهذا الكتاب كما يقول عنه القرشي . مجلد ضخم رأيته وملكته بحمد الله، وله كتاب اللباب، وكتاب الشماع الذي ذكر فيه رأي أبي حنيفة فيمن رفع يديه عند الركوع، وعند رفع الرأس؛ خرجنا بنتيجة مؤداها أن علم أبي المعين ميمون امتداد طبيعي لأسرة عالمة، وأن تلمذته كانت بالدرجة الأولى على كتب أجداده ، وما عدا ذلك فلم أعثر في كتب التراجم على ذكر لأساتذة آخرين تتلمذ عليهم.
This Mu'tamid was born in the year 359 AH and died around thirty-something and four hundred. We add to that that the birth of Abu al-Mu'in Maymun al-Nasafi was in this period, and that his family took the later from the earlier; we concluded that Abu al-Mu'in studied under his father and his paternal grandfather, or at least his books. If we also add that Abu al-Badi' Ahmad was a brother's son of Mu'tamid ibn Muhammad, and we know that he was proficient in fiqh, and that their grandfather is Mak-hul ibn al-Fadl al-Nasafi who has the book al-Lu'lu'iyyat, and this book, as al-Qurashi says about it, is a huge volume I saw and owned by the praise of God, and he has the book al-Lubab, and the book al-Shumma' in which he mentioned the opinion of Abu Hanifa regarding one who raises his hands at bowing and at raising the head; we concluded that the knowledge of Abu al-Mu'in Maymun is a natural extension of a learned family, and that his discipleship was primarily on the books of his grandfathers, and beyond that I have not found in the biographical dictionaries mention of other teachers he studied under.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage provides biographical information about Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi's family background and his early education. It begins by establishing the birth year of a certain Mu'tamid (possibly a relative) as 359 AH and his death in the 430s AH. The author then states that Abu al-Mu'in Maymun al-Nasafi was born in this same period. The key point is that Abu al-Mu'in studied under his father and his grandfather, or at least through their books, as his family maintained a tradition of passing knowledge from one generation to the next. The passage further supports this by mentioning another relative, Abu al-Badi' Ahmad, who was a nephew of Mu'tamid ibn Muhammad and proficient in fiqh. Their grandfather is identified as Makhul ibn al-Fadl al-Nasafi, who authored several works: al-Lu'lu'iyyat (a large volume), al-Lubab, and al-Shumma' (which discusses Abu Hanifa's ruling on raising hands during prayer). The author concludes that Abu al-Mu'in's knowledge is a natural extension of this scholarly family, and that his primary education came from his grandfathers' books, with no other teachers being recorded in biographical sources.

مكحول بن الفضل النسفي
كتاب اللؤلؤيات
كتاب اللباب
كتاب الشماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage says Abu al-Mu'in had no teachers at all.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that he studied under his father and grandfather, and that no other teachers are recorded, not that he had no teachers.

Checkpoint

D) He studied under Abu Mansur al-Maturidi.

Key Takeaway
Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi's education was rooted in his family's scholarly tradition, primarily through his father and grandfather's works, with no other teachers recorded.

?

Traces the scholarly chain linking Nasafi and Maturidi to Abu Hanifa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the chain of transmission linking Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi and Abu Mansur al-Maturidi back to Abu Hanifa, and how the Maturidi school emerged from the Hanafi tradition.

Core Idea
The Maturidi school is a direct scholarly branch from Abu Hanifa, named after Imam al-Maturidi.

This passage clarifies the historical and scholarly connection between the Maturidi school and the Hanafi tradition. It traces two parallel chains of transmission—one leading to Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi, the other to Abu Mansur al-Maturidi—both originating from Abu Hanifa. By examining these chains, we see how the Maturidi school grew from Hanafi roots and eventually gained its own identity through al-Maturidi's preeminent scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall narrative. Then read the translation. Notice that the author is explaining the connection between Nasafi and the Hanafi/Maturidi schools.
The passage uses a comparative structure: first the chain for Nasafi, then for Maturidi.
2
Trace the two chains of transmission
Identify each person in the chain for Nasafi and for Maturidi. Write down the names in order. Notice that both chains start with Abu Hanifa and pass through Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani and Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani.
The chain for Nasafi has additional figures: Mak-hul al-Nasafi and his son Mu'tamid.
3
Find the convergence point
Look for the scholar where the two chains meet. The passage states that at Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani, two branches split: one goes to Mu'tamid (and eventually Nasafi), the other to al-Maturidi.
This convergence explains why both schools share the same root.
4
Understand the naming of the Maturidi school
The passage explains that initially both branches were called the Hanafi school. Later, due to al-Maturidi's abundant knowledge, his name became dominant and both branches together became known as the Maturidi school.
This shows the Maturidi school is not separate from Hanafi but an offshoot that gained its own identity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهنا نصل إلى النقطة الثانية، وهي الصلة بينه وبين الأحناف، والماتريدية، ولبيان ذلك أقول : إن أبا المعالي معتمدًا بن محمد النسفي الجد الثاني لأبي المعين قد روي عن أبيه أبي المعين محمد بن مكحول، الذي روى بدوره عن أبيه مكحول النسفي، والأخير أخذ العلم عن أبي سليمان الجوزجاني عن محمد بن الحسن الشيباني عن أبي حنيفة، فإذا وضعنا بمقابلة معتمد بن محمد النسفي الإمام أبا منصور الماتريدي نجد أن الأخير قد أخذ العلم عن أبي نصر أحمد العياضي عن أبي بكر أحمد بن إسحاق الجوزجاني عن أبي سليمان الجوزجاني عن محمد بن الحسن عن الإمام أبي حنيفة. المنبع إذا كان أبا حنيفة، وعند أبي سليمان الجوزجاني وجد فرعان: أحدهما يأخذ منه معتمد، والثاني يأخذ منه الماتريدي، وعرفا في البداية باسم المدرسة الحنفية، فلما ظهر أبو منصور الماتريدي بعلمه الغزير تغلب اسمه على الفرعين، وعرفا معا باسم المدرسة الماتريدية، وكان ذلك مع نهاية القرن الثالث وبداية القرن الرابع الهجريين، ولهذا نرى أبا المعين يكتب مؤلفاته : التبصرة، التمهيد، وغيرها على طريقة الماتريدي كما هو واضح بمقارنتها بكتاب التوحيد الماتريدي وغيره من كتب الماتريدية.
And here we come to the second point, which is the connection between him and the Hanafis and the Maturidis, and to explain that I say: Abu al-Ma'ali Mu'tamid ibn Muhammad al-Nasafi, the paternal grandfather of Abu al-Mu'in, narrated from his father Abu al-Mu'in Muhammad ibn Mak-hul, who in turn narrated from his father Mak-hul al-Nasafi, and the latter took knowledge from Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani from Abu Hanifa. If we then place opposite Mu'tamid ibn Muhammad al-Nasafi the Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, we find that the latter took knowledge from Abu Nasr Ahmad al-'Iyadi from Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Ishaq al-Juzjani from Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan from Imam Abu Hanifa. So the source is Abu Hanifa, and at Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani there were two branches: one taken by Mu'tamid and the other taken by al-Maturidi, and they were initially known as the Hanafi school. Then when Abu Mansur al-Maturidi appeared with his abundant knowledge, his name prevailed over the two branches, and both together became known as the Maturidi school. And that was at the end of the third century and the beginning of the fourth century AH. For this reason we see Abu al-Mu'in writing his works: al-Tabsira, al-Tamhid, and others, according to the method of al-Maturidi, as is clear by comparing them with al-Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid and other Maturidi books.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage explains the connection between Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi and the Hanafi/Maturidi schools. It traces the chain of transmission from Abu Hanifa up to Nasafi and al-Maturidi. The chain for Nasafi goes: Abu Hanifa -> Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani -> Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani -> Mak-hul al-Nasafi -> Muhammad ibn Mak-hul -> Mu'tamid ibn Muhammad al-Nasafi (the grandfather of Abu al-Mu'in). The chain for al-Maturidi goes: Abu Hanifa -> Muhammad ibn al-Hasan -> Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani -> Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Ishaq al-Juzjani -> Abu Nasr Ahmad al-'Iyadi -> Abu Mansur al-Maturidi. Both chains meet at Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani, forming two branches. Initially, both branches were simply called the Hanafi school. But when al-Maturidi emerged with his great scholarship, his name became dominant, and both branches together came to be known as the Maturidi school. This development took place at the end of the 3rd and beginning of the 4th century AH. The passage then notes that Abu al-Mu'in wrote his major works (al-Tabsira, al-Tamhid, etc.) following al-Maturidi's method, as evidenced by comparison with al-Maturidi's own Kitab al-Tawhid and other Maturidi texts.

Key Terms
أبو حنيفة
محمد بن الحسن الشيباني
أبو سليمان الجوزجاني
أبو منصور الماتريدي
المدرسة الماتريدية
كتاب التوحيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maturidi school is an entirely separate sect from the Hanafi school.
Correction: The passage shows that the Maturidi school originated as two branches of the Hanafi school, both tracing back to Abu Hanifa. It was only later, due to al-Maturidi's prominence, that the name changed to Maturidi.

Checkpoint
At which scholar do the two chains of transmission (Nasafi's and Maturidi's) converge?
A) Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani
B) Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani
C) Abu Hanifa
D) Mak-hul al-Nasafi

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi school traces its scholarly lineage directly to Abu Hanifa through two chains that converge at Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani, and was named after Imam al-Maturidi due to his scholarly influence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on your own chain of knowledge: Who are the teachers from whom you have taken your understanding of Islam? Are you seeking knowledge from reliable scholars connected to the prophetic heritage, or are you relying on random sources? What does the meticulous care these scholars took in preserving their chains teach you about the responsibility of transmitting knowledge?
?
The Maturidi school became known by the name of one scholar, Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, despite both branches originating from Abu Hanifa. Is there a teacher or scholar in your life who has shaped your understanding more than others? How can you honor that influence while remaining connected to the broader tradition?
?
Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi wrote his works following the method of al-Maturidi even though his own scholarly chain did not go through al-Maturidi directly. Are you willing to learn from and follow scholars who may not be your direct teachers, if their knowledge is sound? What does that say about the universality of Islamic scholarship?

Students of Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi
List of known students of Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the known students of Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi and understand their intellectual connections.

Core Idea
Nasafi's students carried his theological and legal teachings across Transoxiana.

Historical records preserve only a handful of names from Nasafi's circle, but each shows how his works influenced later Maturidi scholarship. This lesson introduces the individuals who studied under or were influenced by Nasafi.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overview of the names and their descriptions. Then read the translation.
Notice the names and their associated works or roles.
2
Identify each student
Go through the passage and list each student mentioned. Note any special titles or works attributed to them.
The passage uses footnotes; ignore the numbers for now.
3
Understand the transmission of knowledge
Reflect on how Nasafi's students spread his ideas through their own writings and teaching, especially through his book Tabsirat al-Adilla.
4
Complete the activity
Test your knowledge by matching students to their descriptions in the activity section.

تلاميذه: أما عن تلاميذ النسفي فإن كتب التراجم لا تكشف إلا عن القليل، فيذكر القرشي أن أبا بكر محمد بن أحمد السمرقندي الملقب بعلاء الدين صاحب "ميزان الأصول في نتائج العقول في أصول الفقه قد تفقه على الإمام أبي المعين ميمون النسفي (١) ، كما تفقه عليه أحمد بن محمد بن محمد بن الحسين بن مجاهد البزدوي ابن أبي اليسر، عرف بالقاضي الصدر، وهو من أهل بخاري (٢) . ومن تلاميـ أبو بكر مسعود بن أحمد الكاشاني، وأحمد بن محمد بن أحمد أبو الفتح الحلمي(۳). ـذه الأحناف ونأخذ من مناظرات فخر الدين الرازي في بلاد ما وراء النهر مع الماتريدية أن أحمد بن محمود بن بكر الصابوني صاحب البداية في أصول الدين قد تفقه في علم الكلام على كتاب تبصرة الأدلة لأبي المعين النسفي(٤)، وممن رووا عن أبي المعين أبو الحسن برهان الدين على بن الحسن البلخي (0) كذلك أخذ الفقه (ه)
His students: As for the students of al-Nasafi, the biographical books reveal only a few. Al-Qurashi mentions that Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn Ahmad al-Samarqandi, nicknamed 'Ala' al-Din, author of "Mizan al-Usul fi Nata'ij al-'Uqul fi Usul al-Fiqh," studied fiqh under Imam Abu al-Mu'in Maymun al-Nasafi (1). Also studied under him Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn al-Husayn ibn Mujahid al-Bazdawi, Ibn Abi al-Yusr, known as al-Qadi al-Sadr, who was from Bukhara (2). And among his students are Abu Bakr Mas'ud ibn Ahmad al-Kashani, and Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn Ahmad Abu al-Fath al-Hulmi (3). And we learn from the debates of Fakhr al-Din al-Razi in Transoxiana with the Maturidis that Ahmad ibn Mahmud ibn Bakr al-Sabuni, author of al-Bidaya fi Usul al-Din, studied kalam from the book Tabsirat al-Adilla by Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi (4). And among those who narrated from Abu al-Mu'in is Abu al-Hasan Burhan al-Din 'Ali ibn al-Hasan al-Balkhi (5). He also took fiqh...
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage lists the known students of Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi. The author notes that biographical works record only a few. The first student mentioned is Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn Ahmad al-Samarqandi (known as 'Ala' al-Din), who wrote 'Mizan al-Usul fi Nata'ij al-'Uqul fi Usul al-Fiqh' and studied fiqh under Nasafi. The second is Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn al-Husayn ibn Mujahid al-Bazdawi (also called Ibn Abi al-Yusr and al-Qadi al-Sadr), from Bukhara. Then two more: Abu Bakr Mas'ud ibn Ahmad al-Kashani, and Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn Ahmad Abu al-Fath al-Hulmi. The passage also mentions that from the debates of Fakhr al-Din al-Razi with the Maturidis in Transoxiana, it is known that Ahmad ibn Mahmud ibn Bakr al-Sabuni (author of al-Bidaya fi Usul al-Din) studied kalam from Nasafi's book 'Tabsirat al-Adilla'. Finally, Abu al-Hasan Burhan al-Din 'Ali ibn al-Hasan al-Balkhi is listed among those who narrated from Nasafi, and he also took fiqh (the text cuts off).

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's intellectual legacy was carried forward by a network of students who studied his works and spread Maturidi thought.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Of the students mentioned, al-Samarqandi studied directly with Nasafi while al-Sabuni learned from his book. Which approach do you rely on more in your own learning? Are you willing to seek out a living teacher, or do you settle for books? What might you be missing by not having a direct mentor?
?
This lesson shows that even after Nasafi's time, his written work continued to teach students like al-Sabuni. If your knowledge were to be preserved only in a book, would it be enough to guide someone without your physical presence? What are you actively doing today to ensure your learning is passed on to others?
?
The list of students is short, but their influence reached far. Consider your own circle: who are you teaching or influencing? Are you satisfied with the number of people you guide? What step can you take this week to mentor someone in something you have learned?

The Request That Prompted Nasafi's Creedal Writing
Nasafi quotes his patron's request and a manuscript note identifies the patron.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical context in which Imam Nasafi wrote his creed, specifically the request from a high-ranking patron.

Core Idea
Nasafi's creed was written at the request of a ruler, not independently.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi narrates directly how he came to write his creed. A powerful patron, firm in religion and devoted to the straight path, asked him to set down the beliefs of the early scholars of Ahl al-Sunna. Nasafi considered it an obligation he could not ignore. A manuscript note adds that the patron was likely the Sultan of Samarkand or his vizier.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the overall context. Notice the language of praise for the patron.
The patron's firmness in religion is emphasized.
2
Identify the patron's request
Notice exactly what the patron asked for: a creed from the salaf of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a, explaining their madhhab in tawḥīd.
3
Notice Nasafi's response
See how Nasafi describes his compliance and why he considered it a necessity (lawāzim) that cannot be omitted.
Nasafi uses strong language about the obligation.
4
Consider the historical note
Reflect on the copyist's marginal identification and what it tells us about the relationship between rulers and scholars.
The identification is in the manuscript margin, not Nasafi's own text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد طلب مني من فاز ارتقاؤه إلى أسنى درجة الإمارة والإيالة. واعتلى على أعلى ذروة السيادة والجلالة لصلابته في الدين والتعصب للمذهب المستقيم.... أن أكتب له عقيدة من سلف من مشايخ أهل السنة والجماعة ، وأبين ما كانوا عليه من المذهب في علم التوحيد، فأديته إلى ذلك، ورأيت المعاودة إليه من اللوازم التي لا يجوز الإخلال بها ولا يحل الإعراض عنها. ويذكر ناسخ المخطوط في الهامش أن الطالب كان سلطان سمرقند أو وزيره.
For indeed, one who has risen to the highest rank of emirate and governance, and ascended to the highest pinnacle of sovereignty and majesty because of his firmness in religion and his devotion to the straight madhhab, asked me to write for him a creed (ʿaqīda) from the predecessors (salaf) among the scholars of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jamaʿa, and to explain the madhhab they held in the science of tawḥīd (ʿilm al-tawḥīd), so I complied with that, and I considered returning to it among the necessities (lawāzim) whose omission is not permissible and whose disregard is not allowed. And the copyist of the manuscript mentions in the margin that the requester was the Sultan of Samarkand or his vizier.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is a direct quotation from Imam Nasafi's introduction to his work al-Tamhid, included here by the modern editor to illustrate the circumstances of Nasafi's composition of creedal works. The passage itself begins with the request made to Nasafi by a powerful patron—someone who has attained the highest ranks of political authority through his firm adherence to religion and his dedication to the correct school (al-madhhab al-mustaqīm). The patron asks Nasafi to write a creed (ʿaqīda) representing the beliefs of the early scholars (salaf) of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jamaʿa, and to explain their madhhab in the science of tawḥīd. Nasafi states that he complied with this request, and that he considered fulfilling it to be among the obligations that cannot be neglected or disregarded. Following Nasafi's own words, the copyist of the manuscript adds a marginal note identifying the patron as either the Sultan of Samarkand or his vizier, further grounding the passage in its historical context. The passage thus demonstrates that the impetus for writing a creedal work came from a ruler or high official, highlighting the interaction between political authority and theological expression.

Key Terms
عقيدة (ʿaqīda)
علم التوحيد (ʿilm al-tawḥīd)
أهل السنة والجماعة (Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jamaʿa)
مذهب (madhhab)
سلف (salaf)
لوازم (lawāzim)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nasafi wrote his creed independently without external request.
Correction: Nasafi explicitly states that a high-ranking patron asked him to write the creed; he wrote it in response to that request.

Checkpoint

B) A creed from the predecessors of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a
C) A commentary on the Quran
D) A history of sectarian conflicts

Key Takeaway
Imam Nasafi wrote his creed at the request of a ruler, highlighting the interaction between political authority and theological expression.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Nasafi saw the request to write a creed as a duty (lawāzim) he could not disregard. Is there a religious task you have been avoiding that you now see as a necessity? What would it take for you to act on it immediately?
?
The patron was praised for his 'firmness in religion and devotion to the straight madhhab.' If someone were to describe you, would they say you are firm in religion? What is one area where your faith is shaky, and what concrete step can you take today to strengthen it?
?
Nasafi's creed was not his own invention but drawn from the salaf. Do you seek knowledge directly from the original sources (Qur'an, Sunnah, and the early scholars) or are you content with secondhand interpretations? Which one are you actually doing, and how does that affect your understanding of tawḥīd?

Political Stability in Transoxiana and Its Impact on Nasafi
Describes political and religious stability in Transoxiana under Sunni rulers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the political and religious stability in 5th-century Transoxiana influenced Abu al-Ma'in al-Nasafi's work and thought, as evidenced by his introduction to al-Tamhid.

Core Idea
Stability and patronage enabled Nasafi to articulate Maturidi creed.

This lesson explores the historical context of Nasafi's life. The passage describes the stable rule of Sunni Turks in Transoxiana, and shows how a high-ranking official requested Nasafi to write a creed, demonstrating official support for Ahl al-Sunna. This environment shaped Nasafi's writings and the development of Maturidi theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the historical overview
Read the first part of the passage describing the broader political context of Tughril Beg's death and Nizam al-Mulk's vizierate. This sets the stage.
2
Consider the situation in Transoxiana
Notice the rhetorical question: did similar conflicts occur in Transoxiana? The author answers with a clear statement of political and religious stability.
This stability is contrasted with the conflicts elsewhere.
3
Link stability to Nasafi's life
The passage directly connects the stability to Nasafi's birth and upbringing, claiming it influenced his thought as a Maturidi.
4
Analyze the quote from al-Tamhid
Read the quote from Nasafi's introduction where he mentions being asked to write a creed by a high-ranking official. Note the language of necessity and duty.
The marginal note identifies the requester as the Sultan of Samarqand or his vizier.
5
Draw conclusions
Consider what this episode tells us about the relationship between political authority and theological scholarship in this period.
The author concludes it shows both stability and intellectual freedom for Ahl al-Sunna.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي عام ٤٤٥ه قتل طغرليك، وتولى السلطان ألب أرسلان السلطة، واستوزر نظام الملك الذي ظل في الوزارة حتى سنة ٤٨٥هـ، وبه استقرت الأوضاع لأهل السنة، حيث كان سنيا غيورا على الدين حليما، عادلا، محبا للعلم وأهله (۱). وهنا نسأل: هل حدث في بلاد ما وراء النهر مثل هذه الصراعات؟ وللإجابة نقول : إننا لم نعثر إلا على استقرار سياسي، وديني تعيش فيه هذه المنطقة تحت سلطة الأتراك السنيين من بداية القرن الخامس وحتى بعد نهايته (۲). وتلك الفترة هي التي ولد فيها النسفي ونشأ حيث علمنا أنه بالتقريب كان مولده في النصف الأول من القرن الخامس (٤١٨هـ أو ٤٣٨هـ)، ولقد كان لهذا الاستقرار أثره الكبير في فكره وكتاباته كسني ماتريدي. والعبارة التالية من مقدمة كتاب التمهيد لأبي المعين النسفي تبين رضاء السلطة على أهل السنة في بلاد ما وراء النهر .. يقول النسفي: فقد طلب مني من فاز ارتقاؤه إلى أسنى درجة الإمارة والإيالة. واعتلى على أعلى ذروة السيادة والجلالة لصلابته في الدين والتعصب للمذهب المستقيم.... أن أكتب له عقيدة من سلف من مشايخ أهل السنة والجماعة ، وأبين ما كانوا عليه من المذهب في علم التوحيد، فأديته إلى ذلك، ورأيت المعاودة إليه من اللوازم التي لا يجوز الإخلال بها ولا يحل الإعراض عنها (۳)، ويذكر ناسخ المخطوط في الهامش أن الطالب كان سلطان سمرقند أو وزيره. وأيا كان الطالب فهو دليل على الاستقرار أولا ، وعلى تمتع أهل السنة بالرأي الحر ثانيا في هذه الفترة، وكان علي رأسهم إمامنا النسفي، وهنا نأتي إلى بيان الملامح الثقافية للقرن الخامس.
In the year 445 AH, Tughril Beg was killed, and Sultan Alp Arslan took power, and he appointed Nizam al-Mulk as vizier, who remained in the vizierate until the year 485 AH, and through him conditions stabilized for Ahl al-Sunna, as he was a Sunni zealous for religion, forbearing, just, loving knowledge and its people (1). And here we ask: Did such conflicts occur in Transoxiana? And to answer we say: We have found nothing but political and religious stability in which this region lived under the authority of the Sunni Turks from the beginning of the fifth century until after its end (2). And that period is when al-Nasafi was born and grew up, as we know that approximately his birth was in the first half of the fifth century (418 AH or 438 AH), and this stability had a great impact on his thought and writings as a Sunni Maturidi. And the following statement from the introduction to the book al-Tamhid by Abu al-Ma'in al-Nasafi shows the satisfaction of the authority with Ahl al-Sunna in Transoxiana... al-Nasafi says: "One whose ascent to the highest degree of command and rule was requested from me, and who ascended to the highest peak of sovereignty and majesty due to his firmness in religion and partisanship for the straight madhhab... to write for him a creed from the predecessors of the mashayikh of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a, and to explain what they were upon of the madhhab in the science of tawhid, so I performed that for him, and I considered returning to it among the necessities that are not permissible to neglect nor is it lawful to turn away from it (3)." And the copyist of the manuscript mentions in the margin that the requester was the Sultan of Samarqand or his vizier. And whoever the requester was, it is evidence of stability first, and of Ahl al-Sunna enjoying free opinion second in this period, and at their head was our Imam al-Nasafi, and here we come to the exposition of the cultural features of the fifth century.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is a historical narrative and argument. It begins by setting the broader political context: the death of Tughril Beg, the rise of Alp Arslan, and the vizierate of Nizam al-Mulk, who stabilized conditions for Ahl al-Sunna. The author then poses a rhetorical question and answer: did similar conflicts occur in Transoxiana? The answer is no — the region experienced sustained political and religious stability under successive Sunni Turkish rulers from the beginning of the 5th century AH. This stability is immediately linked to al-Nasafi's birth and upbringing, suggesting that it directly influenced his thought and writings as a Maturidi. To support this, the author quotes directly from Nasafi's introduction to al-Tamhid, where Nasafi states that a high-ranking official — identified in a manuscript marginal note as the Sultan of Samarqand or his vizier — requested that he write a creed based on the teachings of the early Sunni mashayikh. Nasafi complied and considered this task a necessary duty. The passage concludes by interpreting this episode as evidence of two things: first, the overall stability, and second, the intellectual freedom enjoyed by Ahl al-Sunna under the patronage of rulers like the Sultan of Samarqand, with Nasafi at the head of the scholars. The final sentence transitions to the next topic: the cultural features of the 5th century.

The Vibrant Kalām Landscape of Nasafi's Era
Describes the vibrant kalam scene and lists prominent theologians of the 5th-6th centuries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the diverse theological schools and prominent scholars that shaped the intellectual environment in which Imam Nasafi wrote his works.

Core Idea
A clash of schools produces truth — Nasafi wrote in a golden age of kalām.

Imam Nasafi lived during a time of remarkable intellectual ferment. Despite political unrest, the cultural scene flourished, especially in the field of kalām. This passage introduces the major theological schools active in the 5th century Hijri and highlights the key scholars whose works were part of the vibrant debate. Understanding this context helps us appreciate Nasafi's own contribution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the schools
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. List the theological schools mentioned: Ashʿarīs, Muʿtazilīs, Māturīdīs, etc. Notice how the author presents them as actively debating.
2
Locate the scholars and their affiliations
The passage names several scholars. For each, note their school (e.g., al-Qāḍī ʿAbd al-Jabbār is Muʿtazilī). Consider the diversity of figures from different traditions.
Pay special attention to al-Ghazālī as a comprehensive thinker.
3
Reflect on the significance for Nasafi
Think about how this environment of competing schools would influence a theologian like Nasafi. The author implies truth emerges from debate. How might Nasafi's Māturīdī theology fit into this picture?
This lesson sets the stage for deeper study of Nasafi's work.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الملامح الثقافية لعصر النسفي:
عرفنا أن أبا المعين ميمون النسفي ولد وعاش في القرن الخامس وبداية القرن السادس الهجريين.
وإذا كانت الحياة من الناحيتين السياسية والدينية قد حفلت ببعض الاضطرابات فعلى خلاف هذا تماما كان الوضع بالنسبة للناحية الثقافية. فمن المعروف أن حكام الدولة العباسية كان من دأبهم تشجيع العلم والعلماء، ولقد سار على نفس المنوال حكام الدويلات التي انقسمت عن الحكم العباسي بعد ضعفه، بل أربوا عليهم، حيث آووا العلماء وشجعوهم، ولعلنا نذكر صورا مبسطة يتبين منها الملامح الثقافية لعصر النسفي.
فإذا كانت بدايتنا بالعلوم الكلامية نجد أن القرن الخامس الهجري قد حفل بالكثير من الإتجاهات الكلامية، فهناك الأشاعرة، والمعتزلة، والماتريدية، والخوارج، والشيعة والجبرية والقدرية والكرامية، كل يعمل جاهدا على نصرة ما يعتقده بإقامة الأدلة عليه، وبإبطال رأي خصمه وأحسب أن الحقيقة لا تظهر جلية إلا من خلال تصارع الآراء، وأحسب أيضا أن بيئة مثل هذه تتصارع فيها الاتجاهات الكلامية تخرج الكثيرين من الأفذاذ منهم على سبيل المثال: القاضي عبد الجبار (۱) المتوفى سنة ٤١٥هـ، وكان إمام الاعتزال في زمانه، وقدم للمكتبة الكلامية العديد من المؤلفات منها موسوعته الشهيرة في علم الكلام - المغني وكتاب شرح الأصول الخمسة، ومنهم عبد السلام بن يوسف القزويني المتوفى سنة ٤٨٢ه (٢) ، وكان من كبار رجال الاعتزال أمد تلاميذه بتفسير كبير يقع كما قيل في سبعمائة مجلد. ومنهم أبو إسحاق الأسفراييني الأشعري المذهب والمتوفى سنة ٤١٨ هـ (٣) ، وأبو منصور بن أيوب المتوفى سنة ٤٢١ه (٤).
وعبد القاهر بن طاهر البغدادي صاحب أصول الدين والفرق بين الفرق. والمتوفى سنة ٤٢٩هـ ( ١ ) ، وأبو بكر البيهقي المتوفى سنة ٤٥٨ھ (٢)، وإمام الحرمين الجويني صاحب الشامل في أصول الدين الإرشاد والمتوفى سنة ٤٧٨ه (۳). الحسين بن خضر النسفي المتوفى سنة ٤٢٤هـ ( ٤ ) . ومنهم صاحب العقائد النسفية عمر نجم الدين النسفي المولود سنة ٤٦١ه ( ٥ ) .
ومنهم بل وعلى رأسهم جميعا الشخصية الجامعة في عمق بين علم الكلام والتصوف والفلسفة أبو حامد الغزالي المتوفى سنة ٥٠٥ھ (٢). صاحب كتاب الاقتصاد في الاعتقاد، المنقذ من الضلال، وتهافت الفلاسفة، وغيرها.
The Cultural Features of Nasafi's Era:
We have learned that Abu al-Ma'in Maymun al-Nasafi was born and lived in the fifth century and the beginning of the sixth century Hijri.
And if life politically and religiously was filled with some disturbances, the situation was completely the opposite with respect to the cultural aspect. It is well-known that the rulers of the Abbasid state habitually encouraged knowledge and scholars, and the rulers of the states that split from Abbasid rule after its weakness followed the same path, indeed they surpassed them, as they gave refuge to scholars and encouraged them. Perhaps we can mention simplified pictures from which the cultural features of Nasafi's era become clear.
If we begin with the theological sciences (al-ʿulūm al-kalāmiyya), we find that the fifth century Hijri abounded with many theological trends. There are the Ashʿarīs (al-Ashāʿira), the Muʿtazilīs (al-Muʿtazila), the Māturīdīs (al-Māturīdiyya), the Khawārij, the Shīʿa, the Jabriyya, the Qadariyya, and the Karāmiyya, each striving to support what he believes by establishing proofs for it and by invalidating the opinion of his opponent. And I think that truth does not become evident except through the clash of opinions, and I also think that an environment like this, in which theological trends clash, produces many brilliant figures. Among them, for example: al-Qāḍī ʿAbd al-Jabbār (1) died in 415 AH, and he was the imam of Muʿtazilism in his time, and he contributed to the theological library many works, including his famous encyclopedia in kalām – al-Mughnī, and the book Sharḥ al-Uṣūl al-Khamsa. And among them is ʿAbd al-Salām ibn Yūsuf al-Qazwīnī, died in 482 AH (2), and he was one of the great men of Muʿtazilism; he provided his students with a great tafsīr which, as is said, consisted of seven hundred volumes. And among them is Abū Isḥāq al-Isfarāyīnī, of the Ashʿarī school, died in 418 AH (3), and Abū Manṣūr ibn Ayyūb, died in 421 AH (4).
And ʿAbd al-Qāhir ibn Ṭāhir al-Baghdādī, author of Uṣūl al-Dīn and al-Farq bayn al-Firaq, died in 429 AH (1); and Abū Bakr al-Bayhaqī, died in 458 AH (2); and Imām al-Ḥaramayn al-Juwaynī, author of al-Shāmil fī Uṣūl al-Dīn and al-Irshād, died in 478 AH (3); al-Ḥusayn ibn Khiḍr al-Nasafī, died in 424 AH (4); and among them is the author of al-ʿAqāʾid al-Nasafiyya, ʿUmar Najm al-Dīn al-Nasafī, born in 461 AH (5).
And among them – indeed at their head all – is the all-encompassing personality deep in kalām, Sufism, and philosophy, Abū Ḥāmid al-Ghazālī, died in 505 AH (2), author of the books al-Iqtiṣād fī al-Iʿtiqād, al-Munqidh min al-Ḍalāl, and Tahāfut al-Falāsifa, and others.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a descriptive overview of the intellectual background of Imam Nasafi's era. It begins by noting that despite political and religious turmoil, the cultural scene flourished because Abbasid and successor-state rulers patronized scholars. The author then focuses on the field of kalām, listing the major schools active in the 5th century: Ashʿarīs, Muʿtazilīs, Māturīdīs, Khawārij, Shīʿa, Jabriyya, Qadariyya, and Karāmiyya. He remarks that truth emerges from the clash of opinions. He then names prominent figures from these schools: al-Qāḍī ʿAbd al-Jabbār (Muʿtazilī), ʿAbd al-Salām al-Qazwīnī (Muʿtazilī), Abū Isḥāq al-Isfarāyīnī (Ashʿarī), Abū Manṣūr ibn Ayyūb, ʿAbd al-Qāhir al-Baghdādī (Ashʿarī), Abū Bakr al-Bayhaqī, Imām al-Ḥaramayn al-Juwaynī (Ashʿarī), al-Ḥusayn ibn Khiḍr al-Nasafī, and ʿUmar Najm al-Dīn al-Nasafī (author of al-ʿAqāʾid al-Nasafiyya). Finally, he highlights Abū Ḥāmid al-Ghazālī as a comprehensive figure who excelled in kalām, Sufism, and philosophy. This passage sets the stage for understanding the rich intellectual context in which Nasafi wrote his work.

Philosophers and Sufis in Nasafi's Era
Highlights the philosophical and Sufi figures contemporary to Nasafi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key philosophical and Sufi figures that shaped the intellectual environment in which Imam Nasafi wrote his works.

Core Idea
Nasafi's era was enriched by major philosophers and Sufi scholars who influenced Islamic thought.

Imam Nasafi's intellectual world was not limited to kalām; it was also shaped by towering figures in philosophy and Sufism. This passage introduces two of the most influential philosophers of the age—Ibn Sīnā and ʿUmar al-Khayyām—and the great Sufi scholar al-Qushayrī, whose works were part of the scholarly landscape Nasafi engaged with.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation to ensure you understand the content. Notice that the passage divides into two clear sections: philosophy and Sufism.
2
Identify the figures in philosophy
Note the two philosophers mentioned: Ibn Sīnā (Avicenna) and ʿUmar al-Khayyām. List their key works as given in the text.
Ibn Sīnā's al-Ishārāt wa al-Tanbīhāt and al-Najāt are named; al-Khayyām contributed a summary in natural sciences and a treatise on existence.
3
Identify the Sufi figure
The passage then turns to Sufism, highlighting Imām Abū al-Qāsim al-Qushayrī. Note his famous work al-Risāla al-Qushayriyya and its purpose: defense of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jamāʿa.
This shows that Sufism was not separate from Sunni orthodoxy but was seen as a defender of it.
4
Reflect on the implication for Nasafi
Ask yourself: How does knowing these figures help us understand Nasafi's own work? Nasafi likely engaged with their ideas, whether in agreement or refutation.
This passage sets the stage for the doctrinal discussions that follow in the text.

وفي جانب الفلسفة تربعت في هذا العصر شخصية فلسفية تعرضت لمختلف المسائل، وحرصت على إمداد العقول بالزاد الفلسفي العميق في الإشارات والتنبيهات، والنجاة، وغير ذلك، وهو أبو علي الحسين بن عبد الله بن سينا المتوفى سنة ٤٢٨ه (٧) وعمر الخيام المتوفى سنة ٥١٥ه (۸) ، وقد قدم للمكتبة الفلسفية مختصرا في الطبيعيات، ورسالة في الوجود، وغيرها.
أما في التصوف فخير من برع في هذا العصر هو الإمام أبو القاسم القشيري المتوفى سنة ٤٦٥هـ ( ) . صاحب الرسالة القشيرية التي كان من أهم أهداف تأليفها الدفاع عن أهل السنة والجماعة.
And on the side of philosophy, in this era a philosophical figure sat enthroned who dealt with various issues, and was keen to supply minds with deep philosophical nourishment in al-Ishārāt wa al-Tanbīhāt, and al-Najāt, and others. That is Abū ʿAlī al-Ḥusayn ibn ʿAbd Allāh ibn Sīnā, died in 428 AH (7); and ʿUmar al-Khayyām, died in 515 AH (8). He contributed to the philosophical library a summary in natural sciences, and a treatise on existence, and others.
As for Sufism, the best who excelled in this era is the Imām Abū al-Qāsim al-Qushayrī, died in 465 AH (9). Author of al-Risāla al-Qushayriyya, among the most important objectives of its composition was the defense of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jamāʿa.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage continues the survey of cultural features by addressing philosophy and Sufism. In philosophy, the author mentions Ibn Sīnā (Avicenna) and ʿUmar al-Khayyām as the leading figures. Ibn Sīnā is noted for works like al-Ishārāt wa al-Tanbīhāt and al-Najāt, and al-Khayyām for a summary in physics and a treatise on existence. Then, in Sufism, the author highlights Abū al-Qāsim al-Qushayrī, author of al-Risāla al-Qushayriyya, which was written primarily to defend the Sunni tradition. This shows that Nasafi's intellectual environment included not only kalām but also philosophy and Sufism, each represented by major authorities.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The passage merely lists influential figures of the era; it does not indicate acceptance of their doctrines. Nasafi's own work often engages critically with philosophical ideas.

Major Scholars of the 5th Century AH
Lists major 5th-century scholars in theology, philosophy, and mysticism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be familiar with the key scholars of the 5th century AH in theology, philosophy, and Sufism, and understand how they contributed to Islamic intellectual history.

Core Idea
The 5th century AH was a golden age of synthesis in Islamic thought.

This passage from Tabṣirat al-Adilla presents a panoramic view of the intellectual giants of the 5th century AH. The author lists figures across three domains: kalām, philosophy, and taṣawwuf, highlighting their major works and contributions. As you read, notice how he places Abū Ḥāmid al-Ghazālī as the culminating figure who unites all three fields.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of theologians
Identify the scholars mentioned in the Kalām section: al-Baghdadi, al-Bayhaqi, al-Juwayni, al-Nasafi (both). Note their key works.
Pay attention to the death dates provided in parentheses.
2
Focus on al-Ghazālī
Notice how the author singles out al-Ghazālī as the comprehensive figure, listing his major writings. Reflect on why he is placed at the head.
Al-Ghazālī is described as combining kalam, philosophy, and Sufism.
3
Examine philosophers and Sufis
Identify Ibn Sīnā and al-Khayyām in philosophy, and al-Qushayrī and others in Sufism. Consider the diversity of thought in this period.
The passage shows that these fields were not isolated but interacted.

وعبد القاهر بن طاهر البغدادي صاحب أصول الدين والفرق بين الفرق. والمتوفى سنة ٤٢٩هـ ( ١ ) ، وأبو بكر البيهقي المتوفى سنة ٤٥٨ھ (٢)، وإمام الحرمين الجويني صاحب الشامل في أصول الدين الإرشاد والمتوفى سنة ٤٧٨ه (۳). الحسين بن خضر النسفي المتوفى سنة ٤٢٤هـ ( ٤ ) . ومنهم صاحب العقائد النسفية عمر نجم الدين النسفي المولود سنة ٤٦١ه ( ٥ ) . ومنهم بل وعلى رأسهم جميعا الشخصية الجامعة في عمق بين علم الكلام والتصوف والفلسفة أبو حامد الغزالي المتوفى سنة ٥٠٥ھ (٢). صاحب كتاب الاقتصاد في الاعتقاد، المنقذ من الضلال، وتهافت الفلاسفة، وغيرها. وفي جانب الفلسفة تربعت في هذا العصر شخصية فلسفية تعرضت لمختلف المسائل، وحرصت على إمداد العقول بالزاد الفلسفي العميق في الإشارات والتنبيهات، والنجاة، وغير ذلك، وهو أبو علي الحسين بن عبد الله بن سينا المتوفى سنة ٤٢٨ه (٧) وعمر الخيام المتوفى سنة ٥١٥ه (۸) ، وقد قدم للمكتبة الفلسفية مختصرا في الطبيعيات، ورسالة في الوجود، وغيرها. أما في التصوف فخير من برع في هذا العصر هو الإمام أبو القاسم القشيري المتوفى سنة ٤٦٥هـ ( ) . صاحب الرسالة القشيرية التي كان من أهم أهداف تأليفها الدفاع عن أهل السنة والجماعة. وممن عاش في هذا العصر أيضا أبو علي الحسن بن علي الدقاق، وأبو عبد الرحمن السلمي، وأبو العباس القصار.
And 'Abd al-Qahir ibn Tahir al-Baghdadi, author of Usul al-Din and al-Farq bayna al-Firaq, died in 429 AH (1); and Abu Bakr al-Bayhaqi, died in 458 AH (2); and Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni, author of al-Shamil fi Usul al-Din and al-Irshad, died in 478 AH (3); al-Husayn ibn Khiḍr al-Nasafi, died in 424 AH (4); and among them is the author of al-'Aqa'id al-Nasafiyya, 'Umar Najm al-Din al-Nasafi, born in 461 AH (5); and among them—indeed at their head all—the comprehensive figure combining depth in theology (kalam), mysticism (tasawwuf), and philosophy (falsafa), Abu Hamid al-Ghazali, died in 505 AH (6); author of al-Iqtisad fi al-I'tiqad, al-Munqidh min al-Dalal, Tahafut al-Falasifa, and others. On the side of philosophy, in this era a philosophical figure arose who dealt with various issues and sought to supply minds with deep philosophical nourishment in al-Isharat wa al-Tanbihat, al-Najat, and others: he is Abu 'Ali al-Husayn ibn 'Abd Allah ibn Sina, died in 428 AH (7); and ‘Umar al-Khayyam, died in 515 AH (8); he contributed to the philosophical library a compendium in natural sciences, a treatise on existence, and others. As for mysticism, the best who excelled in this era is Imam Abu al-Qasim al-Qushayri, died in 465 AH (9); author of al-Risala al-Qushayriyya, whose most important purpose in its composition was the defense of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a. Also living in this era were Abu 'Ali al-Hasan ibn 'Ali al-Daqqaq, Abu 'Abd al-Rahman al-Sulami, and Abu al-'Abbas al-Qaṣṣār.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of prominent scholars from the 5th century AH (11th century CE). The author lists figures in three categories: theologians (mutakallimun), philosophers, and Sufis. He begins with theologians: 'Abd al-Qahir al-Baghdadi, Abu Bakr al-Bayhaqi, Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni, al-Husayn ibn Khiḍr al-Nasafi, and 'Umar Najm al-Din al-Nasafi (author of the famous 'Aqa'id). Then he highlights Abu Hamid al-Ghazali as the comprehensive figure who mastered kalam, philosophy, and Sufism, listing his major works. Next, he turns to philosophers: Ibn Sina and 'Umar al-Khayyam, noting their contributions. Finally, he mentions Sufis: Abu al-Qasim al-Qushayri (author of al-Risala al-Qushayriyya, written to defend Sunni orthodoxy), and three other minor figures: al-Daqqaq, al-Sulami, and al-Qaṣṣār. The list demonstrates the intellectual diversity of the century.

أبو حامد الغزالي
ابن سينا
عمر الخيام
أبو القاسم القشيري

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These scholars worked in isolation from one another.
Correction: The passage intentionally groups them to show they shared an intellectual milieu, with al-Ghazālī actively engaging both philosophy and Sufism.

Key Takeaway
The 5th century AH produced a remarkable convergence of kalām, philosophy, and taṣawwuf, with al-Ghazālī as its most comprehensive figure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Nasafi's era was enriched by scholars of diverse traditions.

This brief passage introduces three contemporaries of Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi. Though their names may not be familiar, they represent the vibrant scholarly environment of the 5th century Hijri, encompassing theology, Sufism, and law. Recognizing them helps situate Nasafi within a broader intellectual network.

Works of Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi
Enumerates eight works of al-Nasafi with editions and notes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the major works of Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi as listed in a modern edition, including important corrections of common misattributions.

Core Idea
Knowing a scholar’s works is essential for understanding his thought and avoiding misattributions.

This passage is a bibliographical note from a modern editor of al-Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla. It lists eight works attributed to him, along with notes on manuscripts and printed editions. Importantly, it also corrects two common misattributions, showing the value of textual criticism. By studying this list, we gain insight into the scope of al-Nasafi's theological contributions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the editor's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage includes footnotes that are part of the source.
2
Identify the eight works
The passage enumerates several works. Identify each one and note whether it is extant or untraced. Also note the textual notes about copies.
3
Examine the corrections
The footnotes correct two false attributions to al-Nasafi. Understand why these works are not his and who the actual authors are.
This is a common misunderstanding that the lesson addresses.
4
Reflect on scholarly practice
Consider why verifying attributions is important in Islamic intellectual history. How does this affect our understanding of al-Nasafi's legacy?

(2) This: al-Ziriklī attributed to Abū al-Muʿīn al-Nasafī in his book al-Aʿlām the book al-ʿĀlim wa-al-Mutaʿallim, but in truth the book is not by Abū al-Muʿīn but by Abū Ḥanīfa, and Abū al-Muʿīn's role is merely narration. Likewise al-Biyāḍī in Ishārāt al-Marām and al-Ziriklī in al-Aʿlām attributed to him a book called al-ʿUmdah fī Uṣūl al-Dīn, but I found the book is by ʿAbd Allāh al-Nasafī who died in 720 AH, and not by Abū al-Muʿīn al-Nasafī. The book is under number 711 ʿAqāʾid and number 37830 B ʿAqāʾid at Dār al-Kutub, and it is a small manuscript that appears from its topics to be a summary of Tabsirat al-Adilla. And Abū al-Muʿīn al-Nasafī narrated from Abū Ḥanīfa, may Allāh be pleased with them, in addition to the book al-ʿĀlim wa-al-Mutaʿallim, the book al-Fiqh al-Absaṭ and the letter of Abū Ḥanīfa to ʿUthmān al-Battī, the scholar of Basra. And the respected Shaykh Muḥammad Zāhid al-Kawtharī kindly edited the three, and the Anwār Press in Cairo published them in the year 1368 AH.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a bibliographical list of works by Abū al-Muʿīn al-Nasafī, as compiled by the modern editor. It begins with a note about printed editions of Tabsirat al-Adilla and the existence of Bahr al-Kalam in Istanbul libraries. Then it lists eight works: 1) Tasayyud al-Qawāʿid fī ʿIlm al-ʿAqāʾid, noting its two manuscript copies; 2) Manāhij al-Aʾimma fī al-Furūʿ, citing its mention in various biographical works and its presence in Istanbul under an alternative title; 3) al-Ifsād li-Khudʿat Ahl al-Ilḥād, marked as untraced; 4) Īḍāḥ al-Ḥujja fī Kawn al-ʿAql Ḥujja, also untraced; 5) Sharḥ al-Jāmiʿ al-Kabīr, likewise untraced. The footnotes add important clarifications: the first footnote provides information about a commentary on Bahr al-Kalam; the second footnote corrects false attributions to al-Nasafī of two other works (al-ʿĀlim wa-al-Mutaʿallim and al-ʿUmdah fī Uṣūl al-Dīn), identifying the true authors, and notes that al-Nasafī did transmit works from Abū Ḥanīfa, which were later published by al-Kawtharī.

The Chain of Transmission of Tabsirat al-Adilla
Enumerates the seven-narrator chain of transmission of Tabsirat al-Adilla.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the importance of the chain of transmission (sanad) in verifying the attribution of scholarly works, and you will be able to trace the sanad of Tabsirat al-Adilla from Ibn al-Humam back to Shams al-A'imma al-Kurdi.

Core Idea
A book's chain of transmission is its scholarly birth certificate.

In Islamic scholarship, a book's authority depends not only on its content but on its unbroken chain of transmission from author to reader. This lesson introduces the sanad of Tabsirat al-Adilla, a key Maturidi work, tracing it through seven eminent Hanafi scholars. Understanding this chain helps us appreciate the care taken to preserve authentic knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the role of sanad
Understand why chains of transmission are fundamental in Islamic scholarship, especially for works of theology and law. The sanad authenticates the text.
This lesson is a case study in how sanad works.
2
Read the sanad passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the seven narrators listed in order from Ibn al-Humam to Shams al-A'imma al-Kurdi.
The passage uses numbers in parentheses for footnotes, not the order of narrators.
3
Trace the chain step by step
Follow the guided breakdown that lists each narrator. Note their full names, death dates, and key teachers/students. This reinforces the chain's continuity.
Each link connects directly to the next, forming an unbroken chain.
4
Reflect on the chain's authority
Consider how this sanad establishes the book's reliability. Why is it important that each narrator is a known scholar? How does this chain connect to the author (Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi)?
The chain goes back to the author's teacher, but the source mentions only up to Shams al-A'imma.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to verify you know the first narrator and the order. Then, as an activity, write a one-paragraph summary of the chain in your own words.
This solidifies the information.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يطالعنا أول السند ابن الهمام، وهو محمد بن عبد الواحد بن عبد الحميد كمال الدين، الشهير بابن الهمام السكندري (٤) المتوفى سنة ٨٦١هـ، وهو عالم جليل وإمام في البحث من مؤلفاته التحرير في الأصول، وكتاب المسايرة في علم الكلام، وقد تفقه على سراج الدين المعروف بقارئ الهداية. وسراج الدين هذا هو الثاني في سند الرواة، وهو عمر بن علي بن فارس الكناني (١)، فقيه حنفي من أهل الحسينية بالقاهرة انتهت إليه رياسة الأحناف في زمنه، وقد تلقى العلم على علاء الدين السيرافي، وقرأ عليه الهداية. (٢) وعلاء الدين السيرافي (٢) هو الثالث في السند توفى سنة ٧٩٠ه، وأخذ العلم عن جلال الدين الكرلاني. الرابع من رواة تبصرة الأدلة، وهو السيد جلال الدين بن شمس الدين الخوارزمي الكر لاني (۳) شارح الهداية عالم فاضل ، أخذ العلم عن عبد العزيز البخاري. والأخير هو الخامس في سند الرواة. وهو علاء الدين عبد العزيز بن أحمد بن محمد البخاري(4)، وهو إمام في الفقه، والأصول وله كشف البزدوي ، وشرح أصول الفقه، وشرح على الهداية، أخذ العلم عن عمه محمد المرغيناني، وحافظ الدين الكبير. (0) وحافظ الدين الكبير هو السادس في السند، وهو محمد بن محمد بن نصر الإمام حافظ الدين توفى سنة ٦٩٣هـ ، وقد كان إماما زاهدا متقيا جامعا لأنواع العلوم، تفقه على شمس الأئمة محمد بن عبد الستار الكردي()، وهو السابع في السند متصلا من رواة التبصرة، وقد نعت بشمس الدين يقول صاحب الجواهر المضيئة: إنه أستاذ الأئمة على الإطلاق رحل إلى ما وراء النهر، وتفقه بسمرقند (۱) الأعلام للزركلي ج ۵ ص ۲۱۹. (۲) الفوائد البهية للكنوي ص ١٤٤. (۳) المصدر السابق ص ٥٨. (٤) الجواهر المضيئة للقرشي ج ۱ ص ۳۱۷. (٥) الجواهر المضيئة ج ۲ ص ۱۲۱. (٦) الجواهر المضيئة ج ۳ ص ۸۲.
The first in the chain (sanad) is Ibn al-Humam, who is Muhammad ibn 'Abd al-Wahid ibn 'Abd al-Hamid Kamal al-Din, known as Ibn al-Humam al-Iskandari (4), died in the year 861 AH. He is a venerable scholar and an imam in research. Among his works are al-Tahrir fi al-Usul and the book al-Masayarah fi 'Ilm al-Kalam. He studied jurisprudence under Siraj al-Din known as Qari' al-Hidayah. This Siraj al-Din is the second in the chain of narrators (sanad al-ruwah); he is 'Umar ibn 'Ali ibn Faris al-Kinani (1), a Hanafi jurist from al-Husayniyyah in Cairo. The leadership of the Hanafis in his time culminated with him. He studied under 'Ala' al-Din al-Sayrafi and read al-Hidayah to him. (2) And 'Ala' al-Din al-Sayrafi (2) is the third in the chain (sanad). He died in the year 790 AH. He took knowledge from Jalal al-Din al-Karlani. The fourth of the narrators of Tabsirat al-Adilla is al-Sayyid Jalal al-Din ibn Shams al-Din al-Khwarizmi al-Karlani (3), commentator on al-Hidayah, a virtuous scholar. He took knowledge from 'Abd al-'Aziz al-Bukhari. And the last is the fifth in the chain of narrators. He is 'Ala' al-Din 'Abd al-'Aziz ibn Ahmad ibn Muhammad al-Bukhari (4). He is an imam in jurisprudence and principles (usul). He authored Kashf al-Bazdawi, a commentary on Usul al-Fiqh, and a commentary on al-Hidayah. He took knowledge from his uncle Muhammad al-Marghinani and Hafiz al-Din al-Kabir. (0) And Hafiz al-Din al-Kabir is the sixth in the chain (sanad). He is Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Nasr al-Imam Hafiz al-Din. He died in the year 693 AH. He was a pious, God-fearing imam, comprehensive in the types of sciences. He studied jurisprudence under Shams al-A'imma Muhammad ibn 'Abd al-Sattar al-Kurdi (), who is the seventh in the chain (sanad), connected from the narrators of al-Tabsirah, and was described as Shams al-Din. The author of al-Jawahir al-Mudi'ah says: 'He is the teacher of the imams absolutely. He traveled to Transoxiana and studied jurisprudence in Samarkand.' (1) Al-A'lam by al-Zirikli, vol. 5, p. 219. (2) Al-Fawa'id al-Bahiyyah by al-Laknawi, p. 144. (3) The previous source, p. 58. (4) Al-Jawahir al-Mudi'ah by al-Qurashi, vol. 1, p. 317. (5) Al-Jawahir al-Mudi'ah, vol. 2, p. 121. (6) Al-Jawahir al-Mudi'ah, vol. 3, p. 82.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage defines the chain of transmission (sanad) of the book Tabsirat al-Adilla, listing seven narrators in order from the most recent back to the author's teacher. The author uses this to establish the reliability of the book's attribution to Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi. The passage begins with Ibn al-Humam (d. 861 AH), a prominent Hanafi scholar and author of al-Tahrir and al-Masayarah. He studied under Siraj al-Din Qari' al-Hidayah, who is the second narrator. Siraj al-Din (d. ?) was a Hanafi jurist in Cairo and studied under 'Ala' al-Din al-Sayrafi. The third narrator is 'Ala' al-Din al-Sayrafi (d. 790 AH), who took knowledge from Jalal al-Din al-Karlani. The fourth is al-Sayyid Jalal al-Din al-Karlani, a commentator on al-Hidayah, who studied under 'Abd al-'Aziz al-Bukhari. The fifth is 'Ala' al-Din 'Abd al-'Aziz al-Bukhari, an imam in fiqh and usul, author of Kashf al-Bazdawi and commentaries. He studied under his uncle Muhammad al-Marghinani and Hafiz al-Din al-Kabir. The sixth is Hafiz al-Din al-Kabir (d. 693 AH), a pious and comprehensive scholar, who studied under Shams al-A'imma al-Kurdi. The seventh and final narrator is Shams al-A'imma Muhammad al-Kurdi, described as the teacher of the imams, who traveled to Transoxiana and studied in Samarkand. The passage ends with footnote citations for the biographical information.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This chain links you to your teachers through seven generations. Who are the scholars in your own chain of Islamic knowledge? Do you even know their names? If not, what does that say about your connection to the tradition? Make a plan today to learn about your teachers' teachers, and thank Allah for them.
?
Ibn al-Humam studied under Siraj al-Din, who studied under another, and so on back to the source. Your faith is built on the trust and labor of countless souls. Are you carrying that trust with care, or are you careless with the knowledge you have? What one change will you make today to treat your Islamic learning as a sacred trust?
?
These scholars were known for their piety and dedication. When you sit to learn, do you feel the weight of their legacy? Or do you take the transmission for granted? Imagine standing before them on the Day of Judgment—will they be pleased with how you received and passed on their gift?

The Chain of Narrators of Tabsirat al-Adilla
Biographical enumeration of the nine-narrator chain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the chain of narrators that authenticate Tabsirat al-Adilla and the key figures in its transmission.

Core Idea
The chain of narrators preserves the direct transmission of knowledge.

This passage presents the sanad (chain of narrators) of Tabsirat al-Adilla, tracing the book's transmission from the author Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi through nine generations of scholars. Each narrator is introduced with brief biographical notes, showing how the book was passed down through a reliable line of Hanafi jurists and Usul scholars. Understanding this chain is essential for appreciating the authority of the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the numbered entries and the biographical details for each narrator.
The passage uses parentheses for numbering; ignore OCR artifacts.
2
Identify the nine narrators
List the names of the narrators in order, starting from Saraj al-Din (second) up to 'Ala' al-Din al-Samarqandi (ninth). Note the relationships: who studied under whom.
The first narrator (the author) is implied as Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi.
3
Reflect on the purpose of a sanad
Ask yourself why the author includes this chain. The commentary explains that it establishes the direct transmission and reliability of the book.
This connects to the learning objective.

He studied under Saraj al-Din, known as Qari' al-Hidaya. And this Saraj al-Din is the second in the chain of narrators, and he is 'Umar ibn 'Ali ibn Faris al-Kinani (1), a Hanafi jurist from al-Husayniyya in Cairo, to whom the leadership of the Hanafis in his time ended, and he received knowledge from 'Ala' al-Din al-Sayrafi, and he read al-Hidaya upon him. And the last is the fifth in the chain of narrators.
