Al-Maturidi's Refutational Works Against Deviant Sects
List of al-Maturidi's refutational works

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will know the range of Imam al-Maturidi's writings, especially his refutations of the Mu'tazila, Rafida, and Qaramita.

Core Idea
Al-Maturidi authored numerous refutations defending Sunni orthodoxy.

Imam al-Maturidi was not only a teacher but also a prolific author. This lesson lists his major works, focusing on those that refute the Mu'tazila (particularly al-Ka'bi), the Rafida, and the Qaramita. These works show his role as a defender of the faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of works in the passage
Read the Arabic passage listing al-Maturidi's works. Notice the variety of titles and the groups they refute.
The list begins with Kitab al-Tawhid and Kitab al-Maqalat, then moves to refutations.
2
Identify the main groups refuted
The passage explicitly mentions refutations of the Mu'tazila (al-Ka'bi), the Rafida, and the Qaramita. Highlight these.
Al-Ka'bi was a prominent Mu'tazili scholar.
3
Note the specific titles refuting al-Ka'bi
Three works are directly against al-Ka'bi: Radd Awa'il al-Adilla, Radd Tahdhib al-Jadal, and a book in refutation of al-Ka'bi's Wa'id al-Fussaq.
The title 'Bayan Wahm al-Mu'tazila' is also against the Mu'tazila.
4
Consider why refuting the Qaramita was important
The Qaramita were a syncretic and politically active sect. Refuting them was crucial for preserving Islamic orthodoxy.
Al-Maturidi wrote two books against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو لم يكن فيهم إلا الإمام أبو منصور الماتريدي ـ الذي غاص في بحور العلوم واستخرج دررها وأتى حجج الدين فزين بفصاحته وغزارة علومه وجودة قريحته غررها حتى أمر الشيخ أبو القاسم الحكيم أن يكتب على قبره حين توفى: هذا قبر من جاز العلوم بأنفاسه، واستنفذ الوسع في نشره وأقياسه، فحمدت في الدين آثاره واجتنى من عمره ثماره. وهو الذي تخرج عليه الفقيه أبو أحمد العياضي في أنواع العلوم والشيخ أبو الحسن الرستغفني وغيرهما من العلماء المتبحرين في العلوم الملية لكان كافيا وعن ثلب رأي هو لذوي العقول والدين زاجرا، ومن رأى تصانيفه ككتاب التوحيد وكتاب المقالات وكتاب رد أوائل الأدلة للكعبي وكتاب رد تهذيب الجدل للكعبي وكتاب بيان وهم المعتزلة ورد كتاب الكعبي في وعيد الفساق ورد الأصول الخمسة لأبي عمر الباهلي ورد كتاب الإمامة لبعض الروافض وكتابيه في الرد على القرامطة يرد في أحدهما أصول مذاهبهم
And if there had not been among them except the Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi—who dived into the oceans of sciences and extracted their pearls, and brought the proofs of religion and adorned its brilliant points with his eloquence, abundance of his knowledge, and excellence of his intelligence, until Shaykh Abu al-Qasim al-Hakim ordered that be written on his grave when he died: 'This is the grave of one who passed through the sciences with his breaths, and exhausted his capacity in disseminating them and drawing analogies, so his traces were praised in religion and he garnered from his life its fruits.' And he is the one under whom graduated the faqih Abu Ahmad al-'Iyadi in various sciences, and Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Rustughfani, and others from the scholars deeply learned in the religious sciences, would have sufficed, and it would have deterred those with intellect and religion from defaming his opinion. And whoever sees his works such as the Kitab al-Tawhid, and Kitab al-Maqalat, and Kitab Radd Awa'il al-Adilla li al-Ka'bi, and Kitab Radd Tahdhib al-Jadal li al-Ka'bi, and Kitab Bayan Wahm al-Mu'tazila wa Radd Kitab al-Ka'bi fi Wa'id al-Fussaq, and Radd al-Usul al-Khamsa li Abi 'Amr al-Bahili, and Radd Kitab al-Imama li ba'd al-Rawafid, and his two books in refutation of the Qaramita, in one of which he refutes the principles of their doctrines.
Source pages p. 559

Commentary
This passage is a eulogy of Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, praising his profound knowledge and mastery of kalam. It describes his scholarly output using metaphorical language: he dived into the oceans of sciences and extracted pearls, and adorned religion with his eloquence and intelligence. The passage records that Shaykh Abu al-Qasim al-Hakim ordered an epitaph for his grave praising his exhaustive efforts in disseminating and analogical reasoning. It then notes that he was the teacher of prominent scholars like Abu Ahmad al-'Iyadi and al-Rustughfani. The author asserts that even if al-Maturidi were the only scholar in that generation, he alone would suffice and deter detractors. Finally, a long list of his works is given, including refutations of the Mu'tazila (especially al-Ka'bi), the Rafida, and the Qaramita, highlighting his role as a defender of Sunni orthodoxy against various deviant groups.

Key Terms
كتاب التوحيد
كتاب المقالات
الرد على الكعبي
الرد على القرامطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which group is refuted in al-Maturidi's 'Radd Kitab al-Imama'?
A) Mu'tazila
B) Qaramita
C) Rafida
D) Khawarij

Key Takeaway
Al-Maturidi's refutational works targeted the Mu'tazila, Rafida, and Qaramita, showcasing his role as a defender of Sunni orthodoxy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson reveals that al-Maturidi dedicated significant effort to refuting deviant sects. In your own life, do you actively learn and understand the beliefs of groups that oppose Sunni orthodoxy, or do you ignore them? How can you better equip yourself to defend the truth when challenged?
?
Al-Maturidi wrote multiple works specifically targeting the errors of the Mu'tazila, Rafida, and Qaramita. What specific false ideas or doubts have you encountered, and have you taken the time to study their refutations from authentic sources?
?
Consider the weight of responsibility that scholars like al-Maturidi carried in preserving sound 'aqidah. Are you content to rely solely on others to defend the faith, or are you willing to invest in learning the proofs of Sunni doctrines so that you can stand firm and even teach others?

Praise of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and His Tafsir
Praise of Imam al-Maturidi and his Tafsir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the high status of Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi as a scholar and the unmatched excellence of his Qur'an commentary, Ta'wilat al-Qur'an.

Core Idea
Imam al-Maturidi was a uniquely gifted scholar, and his Tafsir stands unparalleled.

This passage from Tabsirat al-Adilla offers a heartfelt eulogy for Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, the founder of the Maturidi school of Sunni theology. The author describes the extraordinary spiritual and intellectual gifts that set Maturidi apart, including miracles (karamat) and a unique command of both religious and philosophical sciences. The passage culminates with a glowing tribute to his monumental Quranic commentary.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then its English translation. Notice the elevated language and the sequence of praises.
2
Identify the key claims about Maturidi
List the specific qualities attributed to Maturidi: his special gifts, the respect of his teacher, the awe of onlookers, and the unmatched nature of his Tafsir.
3
Reflect on the significance of 'awtad al-milla'
Consider the term 'pegs of the religion' and what it implies about Maturidi's role. Also note the prayer at the end.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموضوع لإفادة ثلج الصدور وبرد اليقين، لعرف أنه المخصوص بكرامات ومواهب من الله تعالى المؤيد بمداد التوفيق ولطائف الإرشاد والتسديد من الغني الحميد، وأن ما اجتمع عنده وحده من أنواع العلوم الملية والحكمية ما يجتمع في العادات الجارية في الكثير من المبرزين المحصلين ولهذا كان أستاذه أبو نصر العياضي لا يتكلم في مجالسه ما لم يحضر الشيخ أبو منصور، وكان كل من رأه من بعيد نظر إليه نظر المتعجب وقال وَرَبُّكَ يَخْلُقُ مَا يَشَاءُ وَيَختار وكتابه المصنف في تأويلات القرآن كتاب لا يوازيه كتاب في فقه بل لا يدانيه شيء من تصانيف من سبقه في ذلك الفن، وما أحسن ما قال بعض بلغاء الكتاب في وصفه في كتاب فقال كان من أكبر الأئمة وأوتاد الملة، وكتابه في تفسير القرآن فتق عن المشكل أكمامه وقشع عن المشتبه غمامه، وأبان بأبلغ الوصف وأتقن الوصف أحكامه وحلاله وحرامه لقاه الله تحيته وسلامه.
The subject is for providing coolness of hearts and cold of certainty, to know that he is specially distinguished by miracles and gifts from God Almighty, supported by the ink of success and the subtleties of guidance and direction from the Rich, the Praiseworthy, and that what was gathered with him alone of the types of religious and philosophical sciences is not gathered in common customs among many of the outstanding achievers. For this reason his teacher Abu Nasr al-Iyadi would not speak in his assemblies unless Sheikh Abu Mansur was present. And everyone who saw him from afar looked at him with a look of wonder and said: 'And your Lord creates whatever He wills and chooses.' And his book composed on the interpretations of the Qur'an is a book that no book in jurisprudence matches, rather nothing from the works of those before him in that art approaches it. And how excellent is what some eloquent writers said in describing him in a book: 'He was among the greatest imams and the pegs of the religious community, and his book on the commentary of the Qur'an opened the sleeves of the difficult and removed the cloud from the ambiguous, and he explained with the most eloquent description and the most precise description its rulings, its lawful and its unlawful. May God grant him His greeting and peace.'
Source pages p. 560

Commentary
This passage is a eulogy for Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, founder of the Maturidi school of Sunni theology. The author begins by stating that the purpose of this writing is to provide spiritual comfort and certitude, acknowledging that Maturidi was specially favored by God with miracles (karamat) and gifts. The author then highlights that Maturidi uniquely possessed a combination of religious and philosophical sciences that is not commonly found even among the most accomplished scholars. As a result, his teacher Abu Nasr al-Iyadi would not lecture without Maturidi's presence. The passage also recounts that anyone who saw Maturidi from afar was struck with awe, quoting the Quranic verse (28:68) 'And your Lord creates whatever He wills and chooses.' The author then praises Maturidi's magnum opus, a commentary on the Qur'an titled 'Ta'wilat al-Qur'an', stating it is unmatched in its field. Finally, a quotation from an eloquent writer describes Maturidi as one of the greatest imams and 'pegs of the religion' (awtad al-milla), and his commentary as clarifying difficulties and ambiguities, perfectly explaining the rulings, lawful, and unlawful. The passage concludes with a prayer for God's greeting and peace upon him.

Key Terms
تأويلات القرآن (Ta'wilat al-Qur'an)
أوتاد الملة (awtad al-milla)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mention of 'miracles' (karamat) might be misunderstood as claiming prophethood for al-Maturidi.
Correction: In Sunni theology, karamat are miracles of saints (awliya), which are distinct from the miracles of prophets. They are a sign of sanctity, not prophethood.

Checkpoint
Why would Maturidi's teacher Abu Nasr al-Iyadi not speak in his assemblies?
A) Because he was ill
B) Because Maturidi was not present
C) Because he feared criticism
D) Because he was not a scholar

Key Takeaway
Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi was a divinely favored scholar whose Qur'an commentary remains an unmatched masterpiece in Islamic learning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Imam al-Maturidi combined both religious and philosophical sciences. What is one area of knowledge you have neglected out of comfort? Write down a concrete step to study it this week, seeking Allah's guidance.
?
His teacher would not speak unless Maturidi was present—a profound act of humility. Is there someone whose knowledge or character you could elevate by deferring to them? How will you show that respect today?
?
People looked at Maturidi with wonder and recited a verse of awe. If you were to be remembered for one quality after you leave this world, what would it be? What are you doing right now to cultivate that quality in yourself?

Praise of Sheikh Abu al-Qasim al-Hakim
Biographical praise of a scholar combining outward and inward sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the stature of Sheikh Abu al-Qasim al-Hakim as a scholar who combined external sciences with spiritual knowledge, and his universal acceptance by the Muslim community.

Core Idea
True scholarship integrates outward knowledge with inward spiritual excellence.

In this passage, the author singles out a great scholar of the Maturidi tradition, Sheikh Abu al-Qasim al-Hakim, as a model of comprehensive learning. The text illustrates how he was universally praised not only for mastery of theology and law but also for the sciences of spiritual knowledge and practice. This reflection helps us appreciate the ideal of holistic scholarship in Islam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to hear the author's rhythm, then read the literal translation. Let the praise sink in before analyzing.
2
Identify the fields of knowledge
Note the disciplines mentioned: kalam, fiqh, tafsir, and 'the sciences of spiritual knowledge and practice' (ulum al-ma'rifa wa al-mu'amala). What does this combination tell you about the scholar's profile?
The term 'mu'amala' here refers to spiritual practice, not transactions.
3
Observe the universal consensus of praise
Notice the strong language: 'the entire community approved', 'tongues agreed', 'hearts concurred'. This emphasizes his uncontested reputation.
4
Reflect on the holistic ideal
Think about why the author highlights both outward sciences (kalam, fiqh, tafsir) and inward sciences (ma'rifa, mu'amala). What does this say about the Islamic scholarly ideal?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وممن كان على هذا المذهب الشيخ أبو القاسم الحكيم، وهو ممن ارتضاه الأمة بأسرها، وأطبقت الألسنة على الثناء عليه، واتفقت الأفئدة على التعظيم والإجلال له وقد كان جمع إلى ما كان تبحر فيه من الكلام والفقه ومعرفة تأويل القرآن علوم المعرفة والمعاملة فبلغ في ذلك مبلغا سار بذكره الركبان قربا وبعدا وغورا ونجدا وآثاره في الدين مشهورة، ومشاهده معروفة مذكورة، ومساعيه عند أولي العقل والدين مشكورة.
And among those who followed this school was Sheikh Abu al-Qasim al-Hakim. He was one whom the entire community approved, tongues agreed upon praising him, and hearts concurred in magnifying and venerating him. He had combined with what he had mastered in kalam, fiqh, and knowledge of the interpretation of the Qur'an, the sciences of spiritual knowledge and practice, and reached in that a level that travelers far and near, lowlands and highlands, spread his fame. His traces in religion are well-known, his stations are famous and mentioned, and his endeavors are thanked by those of intellect and religion.
Source pages p. 560

Commentary
This passage is a biographical eulogy for Sheikh Abu al-Qasim al-Hakim, a scholar of the Maturidi school. The author states that al-Hakim was universally approved by the entire Muslim community, with all tongues united in praise and hearts agreed on his veneration. He mastered theology (kalam), jurisprudence (fiqh), and the interpretation of the Qur'an, and in addition possessed the 'sciences of spiritual knowledge and practice' (ulum al-ma'rifa wa al-mu'amala), referring to Sufi knowledge. His reputation spread widely among travelers, and his contributions to religion are well-known and appreciated by people of intellect and faith.

Key Terms
أبو القاسم الحكيم
علوم المعرفة والمعاملة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The 'sciences of spiritual knowledge and practice' are often dismissed as merely mystical or superstitious.
Correction: In the Islamic scholarly tradition, these sciences are a legitimate and respected domain of knowledge, representing inner purification and experiential knowledge of Allah.

Checkpoint
Which additional sciences did Abu al-Qasim al-Hakim combine with his expertise in kalam, fiqh, and tafsir?
A) Medicine and astronomy
B) Grammar and literature
C) Sciences of spiritual knowledge and practice
D) Logic and philosophy

Key Takeaway
Sheikh Abu al-Qasim al-Hakim exemplifies the integration of exoteric and esoteric Islamic sciences, earning universal acclaim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says al-Hakim combined outward sciences with inward spiritual knowledge. Which of these two areas do you neglect more? What specific step will you take this week to strengthen the neglected side?
?
Al-Hakim earned the praise of tongues and hearts. Is your life such that those who know you would praise your knowledge and character? If not, what is blocking you from pursuing excellence in both?
?
The community approved of al-Hakim entirely. Whose approval are you seeking most: Allah's or people's? How does this passage recalibrate your intention?

Life of Imam al-Nasafi Maturidi
beginner

The Early Maturidi Scholars: Predecessors of Ash'ari
Nasafi lists early Maturidi scholars and their death dates to refute the charge of recent origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical timeline of early Maturidi scholars relative to Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, and why the claim that the Maturidi school is recent is unfounded.

Core Idea
Maturidi scholarship predates and parallels Ash'ari, not after it.

Nasafi provides a historical argument to counter the misconception that the Maturidi school emerged much later than the Ash'ari school. By listing early scholars and their death dates, he shows that they were contemporaries or even predecessors of al-Ash'ari. This passage establishes the chronological credibility of the Maturidi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument. Notice the regions mentioned and the tone of defense.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the death dates
List the death dates Nasafi gives for al-Ash'ari, Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, and Abu al-Qasim al-Hakim. Also note the general timeframe for earlier scholars.
Most earlier scholars died before 300 AH.
3
Compare the timelines
Compare the death dates of these Maturidi figures with that of al-Ash'ari. See that they are not later but contemporary or earlier.
Al-Ash'ari died in 324 AH; Maturidi died shortly after.
4
Understand the rhetorical conclusion
Reflect on Nasafi's closing question: if Maturidi scholars predate Ash'ari, how can the school be dismissed as recent? This reveals the bias of opponents.
Nasafi calls such rejection obstinacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أئمة بخاري وجميع ديار ما وراء النهر إلى أقصى ثغور الترك وأئمة مرو وبلخ، وأبنت عن جلالة قدرهم وتبحرهم في أصناف العلوم لطال الكتاب وقصرت عن بلوغ المراد وأكثر من ذكرت من قدماء هؤلاء ماتوا قبل ثلاثمائة، وكثير منهم ماتوا فيما بين ثلاثمائة إلى ثلاثمائة وثلاثين وثلثمائة وأربعين. ووفاة الأشعري كان في سنة أربع وعشرين وثلثمائة. وتوفي الشيخ أبو منصور بعد وفاة الأشعري بقليل والشيخ أبو القاسم الحكيم فيما أظنه مات سنة خمس وثلاثين وثلثمائة فهؤلاء كانوا من طبقة الأشعري ومن تقدم من أستاذيهم وأستاذي أستاذيهم ماتوا قبله بزمان طويل. فكيف يرد هذا القول بحدوث العهد ويقبل ما تفوه به الأشعري من الأقاويل بهذا العيب لولا التعنت.
The imams of Bukhara and all the lands beyond the river to the farthest frontiers of the Turks, and the imams of Merv and Balkh—I have spoken of the majesty of their rank and their profound knowledge in the various sciences; the book would be too long and fall short of reaching the desired end. Most of those I mentioned among the early ones died before three hundred [AH], and many of them died between three hundred and three hundred and thirty, three hundred and forty. The death of al-Ash'ari was in the year three hundred and twenty-four. Abu Mansur [al-Maturidi] died shortly after the death of al-Ash'ari, and Abu al-Qasim al-Hakim—I think he died in the year three hundred and thirty-five. So these were of the class of al-Ash'ari, and those who preceded them among their teachers, and the teachers of their teachers, died long before him. So how can this claim of recent origin be refuted, and the statements made by al-Ash'ari be accepted with this defect, if not out of obstinacy?
Source pages p. 561

Commentary
This passage is a historical defense. Nasafi lists the early Maturidi scholars from various regions (Bukhara, Transoxiana, Merv, Balkh) and notes that most died before 300 AH, and many between 300-340 AH. He then gives key dates: al-Ash'ari died in 324 AH, Abu Mansur al-Maturidi died shortly after, and Abu al-Qasim al-Hakim died around 335 AH. Thus these scholars were contemporaries of al-Ash'ari or earlier. Their teachers and the teachers of those teachers died long before al-Ash'ari. Nasafi argues that it is therefore invalid to dismiss the Maturidi school as recent in origin while accepting al-Ash'ari's views, unless one is merely being obstinate.

Key Terms
ما وراء النهر
ثلاثمائة
الأشعري
أبو منصور
أبو القاسم الحكيم
طبقة
حدوث العهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maturidi school developed much later than the Ash'ari school.
Correction: Nasafi shows that many Maturidi scholars died before 300 AH, and that Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and others were contemporaries of al-Ash'ari (died 324 AH), with their teachers dying long before him. Thus the school is equally early.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, when did al-Ash'ari die?
A) 324 AH
B) 300 AH
C) 335 AH
D) 340 AH

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi school has a scholarly lineage that predates al-Ash'ari, making the claim of recent origin baseless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a group or school of thought based on a vague claim that it is 'modern' or 'recent' without actually checking its historical roots? Take a moment to identify one such assumption you hold and decide today to trace its origin.
?
Nasafi argues that rejecting Maturidi because of recent origin while accepting Ash'ari is obstinacy. In what areas of your religious understanding might you be guilty of double standards—accepting one view without evidence while rejecting another for a flimsy reason? What will you do to correct this?
?
If the Maturidi school has such deep roots, what does that imply about your own obligation to seek knowledge beyond your immediate tradition? Will you commit to learning about a school you know little about, even if it challenges your comfort?

Sufi Masters and the Maturidi Creed
Nasafi cites al-Kalabadhi to prove Sufi adherence to the Maturidi creed and defend it from novelty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Nasafi cites al-Kalabadhi to demonstrate that the great Sufi masters adhered to the Maturidi creed, and how this counters claims of novelty.

Core Idea
Sufi masters were not only spiritual guides but also Maturidi theologians.

In this passage, Nasafi provides a powerful witness: the Sufi tradition itself affirmed the same creed he defends. He brings the testimony of Abu Bakr al-Kalabadhi, a respected Sufi scholar, whose reliability and integrity make his report unassailable. This shows that the Maturidi doctrine is neither recent nor marginal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Read the first sentence. Nasafi asserts that most Sufi masters were on this doctrine. Note the strong language: 'their oceans of knowledge were overflowing' etc.
This is a sweeping historical claim.
2
Examine the source
Nasafi cites al-Kalabadhi's book al-Ta'arruf. He then builds al-Kalabadhi's credibility by describing his character.
Why does Nasafi need to vouch for him? Because he is citing a Sufi authority, not a kalam philosopher.
3
Understand the defense
The last part says that a doctrine held by such people cannot be called recent or attributed to few. This is a refutation of the charge of innovation.
Notice the phrase 'لا ينسب إلى حدوث عهد'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا أن أكثر رجال الصوفية الذين كانت بحور علومهم زاخرة وكراماتهم مع فيما بين الخلائق ظاهرة كانوا على هذا المذهب، ذكر هذا عنهم الشيخ العالم أبو بكر بن أبي إسحاق الكلاباذي فيما حكى من مذهبهم وعقيدتهم في كتابه المسمى بالتعرف لبيان مذهب التصوف وهو الموثوق به فيما يروي العدل وفيما يحكي ومن عرف سداد طريقته وصفاء عقيدته ونزاهته وصلابته في الدين وأمانته، عرف أنه لا يروي عن أحد شيئا إلا بعد تثبت ويقين واندفاع الشك والريبة، ومذهب كانت حاله هذه الحقيق أن لا ينسب إلى حدوث عهد ولا يغري إلا من لا حظ له من العلم، ولا يقرن بقلة القائلين بقدم العالم. ومع أن هذا ليس من باب معمر عن الزهري وعروة عن عائشة إنما المعول عليه ما تمهد من الأصول، ورجح من المعقول وقوي من البراهين والحجج وتأيد من الدلائل والعلل. وقد فرغنا عن بيان ذلك كله على وجه لا يبقى للخصوم بعد الوقوف عليها إلا الانقياد إلى الحق أو الاقتران بسمة العناد والمكابرة. والله ناصر ما ارتضاه دينا للعباد وإن رغم أنف أهل الزيغ والعناد.
This: Most of the Sufi masters, whose oceans of knowledge were overflowing and whose miracles were manifest among the creatures, adhered to this doctrine. The learned master Abu Bakr ibn Abi Ishaq al-Kalabadhi mentioned this about them in what he related of their doctrine and creed in his book called al-Ta'arruf li-Bayan Madhhab al-Tasawwuf. He is trustworthy in what he narrates of the just and in what he reports. And whoever knows the soundness of his path, the purity of his creed, his integrity, his firmness in religion, and his trustworthiness knows that he does not relate anything about anyone except after verification, certainty, and the removal of doubt and suspicion. A doctrine whose state is such is truly not to be attributed to recent origin, nor does it tempt anyone except one who has no share of knowledge, nor is it associated with the few supporters of the eternity of the world. Moreover, this is not like [the chain] Ma'mar from al-Zuhri and 'Urwa from 'A'isha; rather, reliance is placed on what is established of principles, what is preferred of the rational, what is strong of proofs and evidences, and what is supported by indications and causes. We have completed the exposition of all this in a manner that leaves opponents after learning it no choice but submission to the truth or being marked with the brand of obstinacy and stubbornness. And God is the Helper of what He has chosen as a religion for the servants, even if the people of deviation and stubbornness are vexed.
Source pages p. 561

Commentary
This passage is a biographical and methodological defense. Nasafi asserts that most Sufi masters, known for their profound knowledge and manifest miracles, adhered to the same creed (the Maturidi doctrine). He cites al-Kalabadhi, a renowned Sufi scholar, whose book al-Ta'arruf is a reliable source on Sufi creed. Nasafi emphasizes al-Kalabadhi's trustworthiness, careful transmission, and integrity, implying that his report is credible. Therefore, this creed cannot be called recent, nor is it supported only by a few. Nasafi then clarifies that the authority is not based on a chain of transmission like hadith, but on well-founded principles, rational considerations, strong proofs, and clear evidence. He concludes that he has already presented these arguments comprehensively, leaving opponents only the options of accepting the truth or displaying obstinacy.

Key Terms
الصوفية
الكلاباذي
التعرف
مذهب التصوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sufis were only concerned with spirituality and did not concern themselves with theology or creed.
Correction: This passage shows that major Sufi masters were committed to the Maturidi creed, and their theology is recorded by a trusted Sufi scholar.

Checkpoint
What is al-Kalabadhi's book that Nasafi references?
A) al-Ta'arruf li-Bayan Madhhab al-Tasawwuf
B) al-Fusul fi al-Ulum
C) Kitab al-Luma'
D) al-Mawaqif

Key Takeaway
Nasafi demonstrates that the Maturidi creed was not a new innovation but was upheld by the great Sufi masters, as recorded by al-Kalabadhi.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a religious teaching solely because of the reputation of its teacher, without verifying the creed behind it? How does al-Kalabadhi's model of verification challenge you to be more intentional in seeking truth?
?
Nasafi lists specific qualities of al-Kalabadhi: sound path, pure creed, integrity, firmness, and trustworthiness. Which of these is most lacking in your own life, and what concrete step will you take this week to cultivate it?
?
If someone attacked a core belief of yours as an 'innovation,' would you be able to defend it with the same depth of evidence as Nasafi? What one source could you point to, and what makes it trustworthy?

The Methodological Foundation: Rational Proofs over Chains
Nasafi explains that kalam arguments are based on rational principles, not hadith chains.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that kalam derives its authority from rational principles and evidence, not from transmission chains like hadith.

Core Idea
Kalam's authority is built on reason, not chains of transmission.

Nasafi now shifts from historical citation to methodological principle. He distinguishes the proof structure of kalam from that of hadith. While hadith relies on chains like Ma'mar from al-Zuhri, kalam depends on established principles, rational arguments, and strong proofs. This is a key insight into how Maturidi theology establishes its doctrines.

Guided Path
1
Understand the contrast
Nasafi says 'this is not like Ma'mar from al-Zuhri'. He is contrasting kalam with hadith. Identify what is different.
Hadith relies on chains (isnad), kalam relies on rational arguments (ma'qul).
2
List the four bases
He mentions four things: 'what is established of principles, what is preferred of the rational, what is strong of proofs and evidences, what is supported by indications and causes.' These are the foundations of kalam.
They are al-usul, al-ma'qul, al-barahin, al-'ilal.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Nasafi claims he has fully explained these arguments such that opponents have no choice but to submit or be marked as obstinate. This shows confidence in the rational case.
This is a strong rhetorical ending.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Physical Description of the Prophet (Shamail)
Describes the Prophet's physical appearance as narrated by Hind and Umm Ma'bad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed physical description of the Prophet (peace be upon him) as narrated by his companions and its significance as an indicator of his prophethood.

Core Idea
The Prophet's physical perfection points to his spiritual truth.

This lesson presents a detailed description of the Prophet Muhammad's physical appearance, transmitted from Hind ibn Abi Hala and corroborated by Umm Ma'bad. Such descriptions are not mere biography; they serve as part of the evidence for his prophethood, as his physical beauty and noble features reflect his inner perfection. The passage invites you to reflect on how the Prophet's very physique bears witness to his mission.

Guided Path
1
Read the description carefully
Go through the Arabic list of physical traits. Notice the sequence: height, head, hair, skin, forehead, eyebrows, nose, eyes, beard, cheeks, mouth, teeth, radiance, chest, belly, joints, shoulders, forearms, hands, fingers, overall build. Each word crafts a picture of balanced beauty.
Some terms like 'azhar' and 'aqnā' have specific meanings.
2
Identify the narrators
The passage mentions two narrators: Hind ibn Abi Hala (the Prophet's stepson) and Umm Ma'bad (a woman who hosted the Prophet during migration). Their descriptions agree. Why is multiple testimony important?
This strengthens the reliability of the description.
3
Connect to prophethood
The author places this description in a theological work. Think: how does a physical description serve as evidence of prophethood? What does it tell us about the Prophet's truthfulness?
The perfection of creation indicates the perfection of the creator's purpose.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Consider that shamail (physical descriptions) are not just for admiration. They help us recognize the Prophet and affirm his prophethood. This passage prepares the reader for the next arguments about his moral character and miracles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصفه على التفصيل أنه عليه السلام كان أطول من المربوع، وأقصر من المشذب، عظيم الهامة، رجل الشعر، أزهر اللون، واسع الجبين، أزج الحواجب، أقنى العرنين، يحسبه من لم يتأمله أشم، في عينيه دبح، وفي سطع، وفي لحيته كثافة، سهل الخدين، ضليع الفم، مفلج الأسنان، ظاهر الوضاءة، عظيم الصدر، سواء البطن، ضخم الكراديس، بعيد ما بين المنكبين، دقيق المسبوبة، طويل الزندين رحب الراحة، شثن الكفين سائل الأطراف بادن متماسك، وسيم قسيم فخم مفخم ليس بالجافي ولا المهين. وصفه بهذا هند بن أبي هالة عليه السلام - إذ هو كان ابن خديجة رضي الله عنها من أبي هالة بين يدي المهاجرين والأنصار بهذه الحلية - وكان وصافًا - فصدق بها. وكذا وصفته أم معبد لزوجها بما يضاهي هذا فلم ينكر عليها.
He described him in detail: that he (peace be upon him) was taller than the medium-height, shorter than the tall slender, large-headed, flowing hair, bright white with a reddish tint, wide forehead, arched eyebrows, aquiline nose, so that one who did not reflect on him would think him high-nosed, in his eyes a slight redness, in his (chest?) whiteness, in his beard thickness, smooth cheeks, wide mouth, spaced teeth, apparent radiance, large chest, flat belly, large joints, far between the shoulders, fine forearms, long forearms, wide palms, thick-fingered hands, large extremities, well-built, handsome, beautiful, magnificent, majestic, neither harsh nor lowly. Hind ibn Abi Hala described him with this — as he was the son of Khadija (may Allah be pleased with her) from Abu Hala — before the emigrants and the helpers with this adornment — and he was a describer — so they confirmed it. And similarly, Umm Ma'bad described him to her husband with something similar, so he did not deny it.
Source pages p. 720

Commentary
This passage is a descriptive narration of the Prophet's physical appearance (shamail), transmitted from Hind ibn Abi Hala and Umm Ma'bad. The author first presents a detailed list of physical traits: height, head size, hair texture, skin color, eyebrows, nose, eyes, beard, cheeks, mouth, teeth, radiance, chest, abdomen, joints, shoulders, forearm, hands, fingers, and overall form. Each attribute contributes to a picture of balanced, perfect beauty. The description is attributed to Hind ibn Abi Hala, a stepson of the Prophet, who recited it before the companions, and they confirmed its accuracy. Then Umm Ma'bad's similar description is mentioned as corroboration. The passage serves as evidence of the Prophet's noble qualities, preparing the reader for the next argument that such physical perfection indicates truthfulness.

Correction: The author includes it as part of the evidence for prophethood: his physical perfection aligns with his moral perfection, and the unanimous testimony of witnesses strengthens the case.

Al-Maturidi's Criteria for Imamate: Piety and Insight
Al-Maturidi states that imamate should be given to the most pious and knowledgeable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four qualities that Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi identifies as essential for the imamate and how they emphasize merit over lineage.

Core Idea
Leadership in Islam requires piety, insight, and knowledge, not just lineage.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi presents his normative religious criteria for selecting an imam. He lists four personal qualities that should be the primary considerations. This reflects his emphasis on capability and righteousness in leadership.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow. Notice that it begins with 'رأي لأبي منصور الماتريدي' indicating it is his personal opinion.
Pay attention to the four qualities listed.
2
Identify the four qualities
Find and list the four attributes: الأتقى (most pious), الأورع (most abstinent), الأبصر بالأمور (most insightful in affairs), الأعلم بالمصالح (most knowledgeable of interests). Understand each term.
These qualities form the core of Maturidi's criteria.
3
Understand the phrase 'min tariq al-din'
This phrase indicates that the criteria are derived from the religious perspective, not mere political expediency. Reflect on why he emphasizes this.
It grounds the selection in Islamic values.
4
Consider the Quraysh condition
The commentary notes that Maturidi does not explicitly reject the Quraysh condition but adds merit-based qualifications. Think about how this balances tradition and capability.
This is a common point of discussion in Islamic political thought.
5
Reflect on implications
Consider how these criteria would affect leadership selection today. What does it mean to be 'most insightful in affairs' and 'most knowledgeable of public interests'?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رأي لأبي منصور الماتريدي: وشيخنا أبو منصور الماتريدي قال: كان ينبغي من طريق الدين أن ينظر إلى الأتقى والأورع والأبصر بالأمور والأعلم بالمصالح فتعقد له الإمامة من كان هو.
Opinion of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi: and our master Abu Mansur al-Maturidi said: It should be, from the way of religion, to look at the most pious, the most abstinent, the most insightful in affairs, and the most knowledgeable of interests, and then contract the imamate to whomever is that.
Source pages p. 1113

Commentary
This passage presents the opinion of Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi on the criteria for imamate in the Muslim community. He states that from a religious perspective (min tariq al-din), the selection of an imam should be based on four personal qualities: al-atqa (the most pious), al-awra' (the most abstinent), al-absar bi al-umur (the most insightful in affairs), and al-a'lam bi al-masalih (the most knowledgeable about public benefits). The phrase 'min tariq al-din' indicates that this is a normative religious principle, not merely a political recommendation. The passage does not explicitly negate the Quraysh condition mentioned earlier, but instead adds a qualitative dimension that emphasizes merit over mere lineage. The concluding phrase 'fa tu'qad lahu al-imamah man kana huwa' (then contract the imamate to whomever is that) implies that such a person, regardless of other considerations, deserves the imamate. This opinion highlights Maturidi's concern for leadership based on capability and righteousness.

Key Terms
الأتقى
الأورع
الأبصر بالأمور
الأعلم بالمصالح
الإمامة
أبو منصور الماتريدي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Maturidi completely rejects the requirement that the imam be from the Quraysh tribe.
Correction: Maturidi does not explicitly negate the Quraysh condition; rather, he adds qualitative criteria that emphasize personal merit. The passage should be understood as supplementing, not necessarily replacing, lineage considerations.

Checkpoint
According to Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, what qualities should be considered when choosing an imam?
A) Wealth, lineage, and tribal influence
B) Piety, abstinence, insight into affairs, and knowledge of public interests
C) Age, physical strength, and eloquence
D) Political alliances and military power

Key Takeaway
Al-Maturidi argues that the imam should be chosen based on piety, abstinence, insight, and knowledge of public interests, prioritizing personal merit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the leaders you follow—whether in your community, workplace, or family—how often do you measure them by piety and insight rather than popularity or status? Where do you need to realign your own standards with al-Maturidi's criteria?
?
Al-Maturidi prioritises 'the most pious' first. In your own life, do you seek leadership by first cultivating taqwa, or do you pursue influence through other means? What would change in your daily choices if you truly believed piety is the foundation of authority?
?
The passage says the imam must be 'most insightful in affairs' and 'most knowledgeable of interests.' Are you currently positioning yourself to gain the knowledge and insight needed to serve others well, or are you content with ignorance? What step will you take this week to grow in understanding of what truly benefits people?

Resolving the Prophet's Burial Dispute
Narrates the dispute over the Prophet's burial and Abu Bakr's decisive hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a dispute among the Companions regarding the Prophet's burial place was resolved through Abu Bakr's narration of a prophetic hadith.

Core Idea
The Prophets are buried where they die – a hadith that settled a major dispute.

This lesson recounts a sensitive moment after the Prophet's death. The Companions disagreed on where to bury him. Some suggested Jerusalem, others Medina. The issue was resolved by Abu Bakr who recalled a key hadith. Let's read the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the narrative voice come through.
Pay attention to the different opinions mentioned.
2
Identify the different opinions
Notice the two main suggestions: some wanted to transfer the body to Jerusalem, others insisted on Medina. The Ansar's argument is based on Medina's status as the city of emigration.
3
Understand the hadith's role
Abu Bakr's narration provides a general rule: prophets are buried where they die. This rule directly applies to the Prophet's situation.
The hadith is quoted in the passage: 'الأنبياء يدفنون حيث يقبضون'.
4
Reflect on the resolution
The Companions accepted the hadith, and the dispute ended. This demonstrates the authority of prophetic tradition and the unified response of the Companions.
The author highlights 'ببركته' – by his blessing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منشؤه وبلد آبائه وبعضهم أشاروا إلى نقله إلى مقابر الأنبياء ببيت المقدس، والأنصار قالوا إنه يدفن بالمدينة التي هي دار هجرته ومكان ظهور ملته وانتشار دعوته إلى أن روى أبو بكر الصديق الله - "الأنبياء يدفنون حيث يقبضون" - فقبلوا روايته وارتفع الخلاف ببركته.
His birthplace and the land of his fathers, and some indicated his transfer to the graves of the prophets in Jerusalem, and the Helpers said he should be buried in Medina, which is the city of his emigration and the place of the emergence of his religion and the spread of his call, until Abu Bakr al-Siddiq narrated: 'The prophets are buried where they die,' so they accepted his narration and the disagreement ceased by his blessing.
Source pages p. 1135

Commentary
This passage recounts the historical dispute that arose among the Companions regarding the burial place of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him). Some suggested transferring his body to the graves of the prophets in Jerusalem, while the Helpers (Ansar) argued that he should be buried in Medina, his city of emigration and the place where his religion emerged and his call spread. The disagreement was resolved when Abu Bakr al-Siddiq reported that he heard the Prophet say, 'The prophets are buried where they die.' The Companions accepted his narration, and the dispute ended by his blessing. The author highlights the decisive role of Abu Bakr's knowledge in settling the matter.

Key Terms
الصديق
مقابر الأنبياء
دار الهجرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What resolved the dispute over the Prophet's burial place?
A) The Ansar insisted on Medina.
B) Abu Bakr narrated a hadith that prophets are buried where they die.
C) The Muhajirun decided on Jerusalem.
D) The Prophet left a will.

Key Takeaway
The dispute over the Prophet's burial was resolved by Abu Bakr's hadith that prophets are buried where they die.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a disagreement with fellow Muslims, do you immediately turn to the Quran and Sunnah as the ultimate arbiter, or do you rely on your own reasoning? What would change in your approach if you truly believed that prophetic guidance can settle any dispute, just as it did for the Companions?
?
Consider a recent conflict in your life—personal or communal—where emotions ran high. Were you willing to accept a clear proof from revelation, like the Companions did with Abu Bakr's hadith, even if it went against your initial preference? If not, what prevented you?
?
The Companions accepted Abu Bakr's narration 'by his blessing.' Reflect: Do you recognize the barakah (blessing) that comes from submitting to prophetic knowledge? In what area of your life are you withholding submission to a clear command of Allah and His Messenger? What first step will you take today to change that?

Omar's Personal Virtues and Conquests
The author enumerates Omar's personal qualities and conquests as proof of his fitness for the imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments used by classical Sunni scholars to affirm Omar's caliphate based on his personal qualities and conquests.

Core Idea
Omar's caliphate is validated by his unparalleled virtues and divinely aided conquests.

This passage presents a comprehensive defense of Omar’s caliphate by listing his exceptional personal qualities and his historic conquests. The author argues that these attributes are so perfectly combined in Omar that they could not be imagined in anyone else, and the conquests are attested by mutawatir reports. Together, they establish his rightful place as the second caliph.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation slowly. Notice how the author begins by affirming the general qualification (Qurayshi lineage) and then launches into a detailed list of virtues.
2
Identify the two main arguments
The passage has two parts: first, a list of personal qualities; second, a description of conquests. Find the transition word 'وكذا' (likewise) that connects them.
3
Analyze the list of personal qualities
The author mentions over a dozen attributes, from knowledge to asceticism. Consider what each quality implies about Omar's suitability for leadership.
Notice the rhetorical emphasis: 'لا يخطر بالبال تصور اجتماعها في ذات' – it is inconceivable to have all these in one person.
4
Reflect on the role of conquests
The author cites Omar's eastern conquests as evidence of his effective leadership and divine support. Understand the significance of conquering the Persian Empire.
The phrase 'الأخبار بها متواترة' indicates the historical reliability of these events.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[الكلام في صحة خلافة عمر الفاروق]: نقول: إنه كان صالحًا للخلافة لكونه قرشيًا في نسبه – ثم كان في علمه ورأيه وسداده واستقامته وصلابته في الدين وأمره بالمعروف ونهيه عن المنكر وزهده في الدنيا، وظلف النفس عن المعاصي، وقوة بطشه وشدة بأسه، واهتدائه إلى قيادة الجيوش وتهيئة أمورهم، في غير ذلك من الأوصاف التي تطلب في الإمام بحيث لا يخطر بالبال تصور اجتماعها في ذات ولنا عن ذكر كل صفة من صفاته التي هي صفات المدح غنية لاشتهار ذلك، وكذا فتوحه في جانب المشرق من العذيب والحيرة إلى أقصى خراسان وزوال ملك العجم، وانقطاع مدة دولتهم، وانهدام أركان ملكهم، وانقلاع بنيان سلطانهم مشهورة، والأخبار بها متواترة يدخل
[Discussion on the validity of the caliphate of Omar al-Faruq]: We say: He was fit for the caliphate because he was a Qurayshi in his lineage – then in his knowledge, opinion, rectitude, uprightness, firmness in religion, commanding good and forbidding evil, asceticism in this world, restraining the soul from sins, strength of his grasp and severity of his might, his guidance in leading armies and organizing their affairs, and other attributes that are required in the imam such that the imagination of their being combined in one person does not occur to the mind. And we are independent of mentioning each of his attributes that are attributes of praise because of their fame. Likewise, his conquests in the eastern side from al-Udhayb and al-Hira to the farthest end of Khurasan, the disappearance of the kingdom of the non-Arabs, the interruption of the duration of their state, the demolition of the pillars of their kingdom, and the uprooting of the structure of their sultanate are famous, and the reports about them are mutawatir (massively transmitted). They enter...
Source pages p. 1154

Commentary
This passage is a validation of the caliphate of Omar ibn al-Khattab by enumerating his qualifications. The author first notes that Omar meets the basic requirement of being from the Quraysh tribe. Then he lists a comprehensive set of personal qualities: knowledge, sound opinion, rectitude, uprightness, firmness in religion, commanding good and forbidding evil, asceticism, self-restraint from sins, strength, and military leadership ability. The author emphasizes that these attributes are so perfectly combined in Omar that it is inconceivable for all of them to be found in a single person ordinarily. This hyperbolic praise serves to underline Omar's exceptional suitability. The author then states that it is unnecessary to detail each attribute because they are well-known. He further supports Omar's legitimacy by citing his conquests in the East, from the initial battles at al-Udhayb and al-Hira all the way to Khurasan, which led to the downfall of the Persian Empire. These conquests are described as famous and reported through mutawatir (massively transmitted) traditions, leaving no doubt about their historicity. The overall argument is that Omar's personal virtues and military successes together provide unequivocal proof of his rightful caliphate, countering any Rafidi objections.

الخلافة
الإمامة
المتواترة

Correction: The author shows that Omar's caliphate is validated by his personal religious virtues (knowledge, firmness in religion, asceticism) and his conquests, which are divinely aided and widely documented.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author says Omar's virtues are so combined that they 'do not occur to the mind'. Look at your own character: which virtue is conspicuously missing? Will you commit to a specific action this week to develop it?
?
Omar's conquests are attributed to divine aid. What 'conquest' over your own nafs (ego) or a persistent sin are you avoiding? What step can you take right now to ask Allah for victory over it?

Defending Umar: Virtues Refute Injustice
The author enumerates Umar's virtues and cites prophetic praise to refute accusations of injustice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses Umar's virtues and prophetic praise to refute accusations of injustice.

Core Idea
Umar's virtues and prophetic testimony make accusations of injustice untenable.

This passage from Tabsirat al-Adilla presents a vigorous defense of Umar ibn al-Khattab against accusations that he acted unjustly in matters of the caliphate and marriage. The author lists a cascade of Umar's virtues—from his early precedence in Islam to his asceticism and care for the needy—and supports them with prophetic hadith. The purpose is to show that such a person could not possibly commit injustice, and that those who claim otherwise are beyond rational argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then the English translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
Notice the accumulation of virtues before the hadith.
2
Identify the virtues and hadith
List the virtues mentioned: precedence, effort, knowledge, asceticism, compassion. Then note the four hadith quoted about Umar.
The hadith include 'If there were a prophet after me, it would be Umar.'
3
Analyze the conclusion
The author argues that given these virtues, Umar cannot have committed injustice. Ask why the author then says such a person is 'beyond correction' and requires harsh treatment.
This shows the severity of obstinacy in rejecting clear evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن من ظن به مع سابقته في الدين وسعيه في تقوية الإسلام ثم مع ما له من جلالة القدر في العلم، وشدة الحرص في توطيد معالم الإسلام، وتشييد بنيان الملة ثم مع ما له من الزهد في الدنيا واختيار الفقر ولبسه الصوف واللباس الخشن ما له من الشفقة على اليتامى والأرامل والضعفاء والزَّمنى، والاشتغال بتفقد أمورهم والقيام بمصالحهم، وتهيئة أسباب معيشتهم وسد خلتهم، ثم مع ما له من المناقب حتى قال فيه النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: "لو كان بعدي نبي لكان عمر" وقال: "لو لم أبعث فيكم لبعث عمر" وقال: "إن الله ضرب الحق على لسان عمر وقلبه بقول الحق وإن كان مرا" وقال عليه السلام: "وإن فيكم لمحدثين وإن عمر لمنهم" أمثال لهذا يطول ذكرها ولا يدرك قعرها أنه لا يغصب الحق أهله، ويستولي على ما كان غيره أحق به ظلمًا منه وعتوا ويتزوج ابنته قهرا شاء أو أبى لحقيق أن لا يعد في عداد أهل الدين ويستنكف عن مجادلته، ويتحامي عن إرشاده، ومن هذا منزلته في العلم والعقل غير قابل للعلاج وغير متلق للإرشاد بالقبول والحجة بالانقياد بل تقويمه بالصفع بالنعل وإلا فعلاجه القتل، إذ لا داء أعضل من داء العناد ( وَمَن يُضْلِلِ اللَّهُ فَالَهُ مِنْ هَادٍ
Then, whoever thinks concerning him, given his precedence in religion and his effort in strengthening Islam, then given what he has of majesty of rank in knowledge, and intense eagerness in establishing the landmarks of Islam and building the structure of the nation, then given what he has of asceticism in the world and choosing poverty and wearing wool and coarse clothing, and his compassion for orphans, widows, the weak, and the chronically ill, and his preoccupation with checking on their affairs and managing their interests and preparing means of their livelihood and filling their need, then given what he has of merits until the Prophet, peace be upon him, said about him: 'If there were a prophet after me, it would be Umar,' and said: 'If I were not sent among you, Umar would have been sent,' and said: 'Indeed Allah placed truth on the tongue and heart of Umar, speaking truth even if it is bitter,' and he, peace be upon him, said: 'Indeed among you are inspired speakers, and Umar is one of them' – examples of this too numerous to mention and whose depth cannot be fathomed – that he does not usurp the right of its people, nor seize what others are more entitled to, out of oppression and transgression, nor marry his daughter coercively, willing or refusing, he is truly not to be counted among the people of religion, and one refrains from debating him and avoids guiding him. And one of this status in knowledge and intellect is not treatable and does not receive guidance with acceptance nor argument with submission; rather, his correction is by slapping with a sandal, otherwise his treatment is killing, for there is no disease more stubborn than the disease of obstinacy. And whom Allah leads astray, for him there is no guide.
Source pages p. 1156

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who accuse Umar ibn al-Khattab of injustice, specifically the claim that he wrongfully seized the caliphate and usurped the rights of others, including marrying the daughter of Ali against his will. The author begins by listing Umar's virtues: his precedence in Islam, his efforts to strengthen Islam, his high rank in knowledge, his zeal in establishing Islamic teachings, his asceticism in the world, his compassion for orphans, widows, and the weak, his care for their affairs, and his numerous merits as testified by the Prophet. These include hadiths stating that Umar would have been a prophet if there were one after Muhammad, that Allah put truth on his tongue and heart, and that he is among the inspired speakers. Given all these, the author argues, it is inconceivable that he would unjustly take what is not his right or forcibly marry someone's daughter. Therefore, anyone who attributes such actions to him is not to be counted among the people of religion; such a person is beyond argument and should be disciplined by force if necessary, for there is no disease worse than obstinacy. The passage concludes with the Quranic verse: 'And whom Allah leads astray, for him there is no guide.'

Correction: The author shows that Umar's virtues and prophetic praise make such accusations impossible; thus the accuser is obstinate.

B) To refute accusations that he acted unjustly
C) To prove that he was a prophet

Key Takeaway
The author defends Umar by listing his virtues and prophetic praise, concluding that accusations of injustice are impossible and the accuser is obstinate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author warns that obstinacy is a stubborn disease. Is there any area in your own life—a belief you hold, a habit you cling to, or a person you judge—where you are refusing to accept clear truth despite evidence? What would it take for you to break that obstruction today?
?
This passage uses prophetic testimony as the highest proof of virtue. Do you give the words of the Prophet the same decisive weight in your own judgments about people, or do you allow your own assumptions to override them? How might your relationships change if you prioritized what Allah and His Messenger have said about a person over your own opinions?

Early Caliphs Had Pure Intentions
Refutation of worldly motives through conditional and contrast.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses a hypothetical about Ali and a contrast between worldly and religious motives to argue that the early caliphs were not motivated by power or wealth.

Core Idea
The early caliphs' pure intentions are proven by their character and goals.

Accusations against the early caliphs often claim they sought power and wealth. In this passage, the author presents a powerful hypothetical argument using Ali’s character to show the inconsistency of that charge, then describes the noble motives that actually drove the caliphate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once, then the translation, to grasp the overall flow and argument.
Notice the shift from the hypothetical about Ali to the description of caliphs' motives.
2
Identify the two main parts
The passage has a conditional argument about Ali and a direct statement of the caliphs' intentions. Pinpoint where each begins.
The word 'ثم' marks the transition.
3
Understand the conditional argument
The author lists negative traits that would have appeared in Ali if the accusations were true. Note how each trait is negated.
This refutes by showing the accusers' claims are inconsistent.
4
Contrast worldly and religious motives
The author describes what the caliphs were NOT after (worldly gains) and what they WERE after (strengthening Islam).
The detailed description of worldly seekers highlights the caliphs' selflessness.
5
Connect to the divine promise
The passage ends with a Quranic verse about Allah's promise. Reflect on how this supports the caliphs' motives.
Even though the verse is incomplete, it reinforces divine approval.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كان منه ما زعموا لم يكن لعلي ضعف في البدن، ولا جور في الرأي، ولا جبن في القلب، ولا فتور عن الحمية، ولا قلة في عدد العشيرة ولا خمول في الذكر ولا مساهلة فيما يرجع إلى أمور الدين، ثم لم يكن تقلدهم للخلافة واستيلاؤهم على الإمارة لاستعباد الرقاب، وبسط اليد في الحطام والاحتواء على كل نفيس واجتذاب كل خطير من الأموال كما هو عادة طلاب الدنيا ممن له إرث ملك أو قدم بيت أو من الفتاك والمتصعلكين الذين همهم التغالب والتجاذب، وجمع الأصحاب والترأس على الأحزاب، ليتواصلوا به إلى قهر الأقران والأتراب فتصفو لهم المملكة، ويخلص لهم ما هو بنيتهم من نيل الرغائب والفوز بالمطالب والاستمتاع بصنوف الملاذ وضروب الشهوات، وإعطاء النفس الأمارة بالسوء منيتها وإنالة النفس السبعية بغيتها ونهمتها ليقع به التنازع والتخاصم ويغصب البعض حق البعض، ويستولي على ما هو حق الغير، بل كانت هممهم مقصورة على الاشتغال بما فيه تقوية أركان الدين وإرساء قواعد الإسلام، وإرادتهم مصروفة إلى ما فيه انتشار الدعوة وظهور الملة، وتحقيق ما سبق الوعد به بقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَعَدَ اللَّهُ الَّذِينَ
And if what they claim were from him, Ali would not have had weakness in body, nor injustice in opinion, nor cowardice in heart, nor slackness in zeal, nor fewness in number of clan, nor obscurity in reputation, nor negligence in matters pertaining to religion. Then, their assumption of the caliphate and their control over the emirate was not for enslaving necks, nor for extending the hand in worldly goods and encompassing every precious thing and attracting every serious wealth, as is the custom of seekers of the world, those who have an inheritance of kingship or an ancient house, or from the assassins and vagabonds whose concern is mutual overpowering and contention, and gathering companions and assuming leadership over parties, in order to thereby attain to overpowering peers and equals, so that the kingdom becomes pure for them, and what is their intention becomes exclusively theirs: attaining desires, winning objectives, enjoying varieties of pleasures and kinds of lusts, and giving the commanding soul its wish and granting the animal soul its desire and appetite, so that contention and conflict occur, and one usurps the right of another, and seizes what is the right of others. Rather, their ambitions were confined to occupying themselves with what strengthens the pillars of religion and establishes the foundations of Islam, and their desires were directed to what spreads the call and manifests the nation, and to the realization of what was previously promised in His saying, exalted is He: 'Allah has promised those who...'
Source pages p. 1157

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of accusations against the early caliphs, particularly the claim that they sought worldly power and dominion. The author presents a hypothetical: if the caliphs were as accused (i.e., unjust and power-hungry), then Ali (who was one of them) would have shown physical weakness, poor judgment, cowardice, lack of zeal, few supporters, obscurity, and negligence in religious matters—but none of this was true of Ali; he was known for his strength, wisdom, courage, and religious commitment. Then the author describes what the caliphs' motives were not: they did not take the caliphate to enslave people, accumulate wealth, or enjoy pleasures like worldly rulers do, nor did they desire to overpower others or gather followers for personal gain. Rather, their ambitions were solely focused on strengthening the pillars of Islam and spreading the call to the religion, fulfilling the divine promise mentioned in the Quranic verse that begins 'Allah has promised those who have believed among you and done righteous deeds that He will surely grant them succession...' (the verse is incomplete here). Thus, their intentions were pure and selfless, entirely for the sake of Allah and His religion.

Key Terms
الخلافة
المطالب الدنيوية
تقوية الدين
الإرادة
الوعد الإلهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The early caliphs sought worldly power and wealth.
Correction: The author argues that their character (exemplified by Ali) and their focus on strengthening Islam prove they were selfless.

Checkpoint

A) To gain wealth and enjoy pleasures
B) To strengthen the pillars of religion and spread Islam
C) To achieve fame and reputation
D) To overpower their enemies

Key Takeaway
The early caliphs accepted the caliphate to strengthen Islam, not for worldly gain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you take on a responsibility in the Muslim community, do you examine your intention to ensure it is purely for Allah? Think of a specific role or task you currently hold. Is strengthening the deen your true aim, or does the desire for recognition, influence, or personal benefit secretly drive you? What can you do today to purify that intention?
?
The passage contrasts the seekers of this world with the selflessness of the caliphs. In your daily life—whether at work, with family, or in your worship—which category are your actions closer to? Are you extending your hand for the 'hitam' (worldly goods) or are you focused on the pillars of deen? Ask Allah to make you among the sincere and take one practical step to realign your motive.
?
If someone accused you of being motivated by pride or worldly ambition in your service to Islam, what evidence could you provide from your life to refute that, similar to how the author used Ali's (RA) example? Are there areas where you need to be more like the caliphs—where you need to abandon the desires of the nafs and dedicate yourself solely to the spread of this deen? Make a concrete commitment to do so today.

The Principle of Following the Perfect
Explains why Abu Bakr is the one followed despite earlier believers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Abu Bakr is considered the one followed (al-muqtađā) despite others believing earlier, based on his complete virtues of intellect, knowledge, and sound judgment.

Core Idea
People follow the perfect, not the first—Abu Bakr's complete virtues made him the leader.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi argues that chronological precedence alone does not determine leadership in Islam. Instead, the one who possesses complete intellectual, knowledgeable, and judgmental qualities is worthy of being followed. Sanusi presents this principle to establish Abu Bakr's unique status, despite the earlier faith of Khadija, Ali, and Zayd.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on reward for guiding
Start by reading the opening lines about how guiding someone to faith earns the reward of that person's future believers. This sets up why Abu Bakr's efforts are so significant.
Notice the claim that Umar himself is a 'good deed' from Abu Bakr's good deeds.
2
Identify the order of first believers
The text lists the first believers in each category: women, boys, slaves, free men. Observe that Abu Bakr is last among them in chronological order.
This challenges the idea that being first makes one most virtuous.
3
Understand the principle of 'al-muqtađā'
Read why people do not follow women, boys, or slaves but follow one whose merits are complete. The key qualities are abundance of intellect, perfection of knowledge, and soundness of opinion.
This is the core argument: completeness of virtues matters more than chronological order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحسنة، وهو تصديق رسول الله عليه السلام فيكون له مثل أجر من آمن به إلى يوم القيامة، وعن هذا قالوا: إن عمر الله مع جلال قدره وكثرة مناقبه ومحله الشريف في الإسلام، كان حسنة من حسنات أبي بكر الصديق - رضي الله عنهما – ثم من تورع منهم فقال : أول من آمن بالنبي من النساء خديجة، ومن الصبيان علي ومن العبيد زيد بن حارثة ، ومن الرجال الأحرار أبو بكر الصديق - رضي الله عنهم أجمعين - ثم كيفما كان فهو المقتدى لغيره في الإسلام إذ الناس لا يقتدرون بالإناث والصبيان والعبيد إنما يقتدرون بمن تمت مناقبة، واشتهر بوفور العقل وكمال العلم وأصالة الرأي، ولهذا لم يقتد أحد بمن سوى أبي بكر الله في ذلك بل اقتدوا به حتى آمن يوم إسلامه أو غده: عثمان بن عفان، والزبير بن العوام، وطلحة بن عبيد الله وعبد الرحمن بن عوف، وسعد بن أبي وقاص – رضي الله عنهم فجاء بهم إلى رسول الله الله فعرض عليهم الإسلام، وقرأ عليهم القرآن فآمنوا به وصدقوا برسالته وقيل لما أسلم أبو بكر وانصرف من عند رسول الله راح عليه بعثمان بن عفان ولطلحة بن عبيد الله والزبير بن العوام وسعد بن أبي وقاص، ثم جاء الغد بعثمان بن مظعون وبأبي عبيدة بن الجراح، وعبد الرحمن بن عوف، وبأبي سلمى بن الأسد والأرقم بن أبي الأرقم - رضي الله عنهم
The good deed, and it is believing in the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, so he will have the reward of those who believe in him until the Day of Resurrection. And on this they said: Indeed, ʿUmar (may Allah be pleased with him), despite his great status, abundant merits, and noble position in Islam, was a good deed from the good deeds of Abū Bakr al-Ṣiddīq (may Allah be pleased with them both). Then those who were cautious among them said: The first to believe in the Prophet among women was Khadījah, among boys was ʿAlī, among slaves was Zayd ibn Ḥārithah, and among free men was Abū Bakr al-Ṣiddīq (may Allah be pleased with them all). Then however it was, he (Abū Bakr) is the one followed by others in Islam, for people do not follow [women, boys, and slaves]; rather they follow one whose merits are complete, and who is famous for abundance of intellect, perfection of knowledge, and soundness of opinion. For this reason, no one followed anyone other than Abū Bakr in that regard; rather they followed him, until there believed on the day of his Islam or the next: ʿUthmān ibn ʿAffān, al-Zubayr ibn al-ʿAwwām, Ṭalḥah ibn ʿUbaydillāh, ʿAbd al-Raḥmān ibn ʿAwf, and Saʿd ibn Abī Waqqāṣ (may Allah be pleased with them). So he brought them to the Messenger of Allah, and he presented Islam to them, and recited the Quran to them, so they believed in him and affirmed his message. And it is said: When Abū Bakr embraced Islam and returned from the Messenger of Allah, he went to ʿUthmān ibn ʿAffān, Ṭalḥah ibn ʿUbaydillāh, al-Zubayr ibn al-ʿAwwām, and Saʿd ibn Abī Waqqāṣ; then the next day he came with ʿUthmān ibn Maẓʿūn, Abū ʿUbaydah ibn al-Jarrāḥ, ʿAbd al-Raḥmān ibn ʿAwf, Abū Salamā? (or Abū Salmā) ibn al-Asad, and al-Arqam ibn Abī al-Arqam - may Allah be pleased with them.
Source pages p. 1188

Commentary
This passage is a historical and theological argument for the precedence of Abū Bakr al-Ṣiddīq. It begins by stating that believing in the Messenger is a good deed that brings reward equal to those who believe through that person, establishing the concept that guiding others to faith earns one their reward. It then uses the example of ʿUmar ibn al-Khaṭṭāb, despite his great status, being a 'good deed' from Abū Bakr's good deeds, implying that Abū Bakr's role in bringing people to Islam (including ʿUmar) gave him immense reward. Next, it addresses the question of who was the first believer in various categories (women, boys, slaves, free men) to acknowledge that chronologically, others preceded Abū Bakr in some regards. However, the key point is that Abū Bakr is the one who is followed (al-muqtađā) because he possessed complete virtues: intellect, knowledge, and sound judgment. People do not follow women, boys, or slaves, no matter how early they believed, but rather follow a perfect individual. Therefore, the major companions followed Abū Bakr, embracing Islam on the day he became Muslim or soon after, and through his efforts, the early prominent converts (like ʿUthmān, al-Zubayr, Ṭalḥah, ʿAbd al-Raḥmān ibn ʿAwf, Saʿd, and others) came to the Prophet. The passage then describes how Abū Bakr personally brought these individuals to the Prophet. The overall argument is that despite chronological precedence of others, Abū Bakr's complete merits made him the effective leader in attracting converts, establishing his high rank in Islam.

Key Terms
المقتدى
مناقبة
وفور العقل
كمال العلم
أصالة الرأي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first believer chronologically is the most virtuous and should be followed.
Correction: The passage teaches that being first does not guarantee being followed; instead, complete virtues (intellect, knowledge, sound judgment) determine who is followed.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did people follow Abū Bakr instead of earlier believers like Khadīja or ʿAlī?
A) Because he was the first free man to believe.
B) Because his merits were complete, and he was known for intellect, knowledge, and sound opinion.
C) Because he brought ʿUmar to Islam.
D) Because he was older than the other believers.

Key Takeaway
Abū Bakr's precedence is based on his complete virtues, not his chronological order in believing, making him the one followed in Islam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your life, who do you look up to and why? Is it because of their complete virtues or simply because they were the first to influence you? How does this lesson challenge your criteria for choosing role models?
?
You have the power to guide others to faith. If someone you guide becomes a great believer like Umar, would you be content with seeing them as your 'good deed'? What does this teach you about humility and the true reward for guiding?
?
Are you content with being a follower of Abu Bakr, or do you secretly wish to be the first in something? How does the principle of following the perfect reshape your ambitions and your view of leadership?

Abu Bakr's Role in Early Conversions
Lists early converts and narrates Abu Bakr's role in bringing them to the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu Bakr's efforts led to the conversion of several key companions on the day of his Islam or shortly after.

Core Idea
Abu Bakr's personal invitations brought the first wave of influential converts to the Prophet.

After establishing that Abu Bakr is the one followed due to his complete virtues, the passage provides concrete evidence: a list of companions who embraced Islam on the day of Abu Bakr's conversion or the next day. The detailed narrative shows Abu Bakr actively bringing these individuals to the Prophet, demonstrating his pivotal role in the early expansion of Islam.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of early converts
Start by reading the names: ʿUthmān, al-Zubayr, Ṭalḥah, ʿAbd al-Raḥmān ibn ʿAwf, Saʿd ibn Abī Waqqāṣ. Note that these are among the most prominent companions.
These are the 'Ten Promised Paradise' except Abū Bakr and ʿUmar.
2
Understand the timing of their conversion
The text says they believed 'on the day of his Islam or the next.' This shows immediate influence.
Abū Bakr’s conversion sparked a chain reaction.
3
Review the narrative of Abu Bakr's actions
Read how Abū Bakr personally went to these individuals and brought them to the Prophet, who then presented Islam to them.
This highlights his proactive role as a da’wah leader.

?

?

?
Abu Bakr didn't wait—he acted the same day or the next. Is there a hesitation in your heart to share the truth you know? What fear or comfort is preventing you from being as proactive as Abu Bakr in guiding others?
